"THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
‘‘Tlae Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis-
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
One Person, one Vote.
Complete Secularisation and Freedom of State
Education,
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
Australia for the Australians.—The cheap
Chinaman, the cheap nigger, and the
cheap European pauper to be absolutely
excluded.
A State Bank, the issue of bank-notes to be a
State monopoly.
The election of Ministers by Parlia-
ment, instead of Party Government or
rather Government by Contradiction.
A new Parliamentary System—one House to
be elected by constituencies as at present ;
the other to be chosen by the whole
country voting as one Constituency,
A Universal System of Compulsory Life
Insurance.
The entire Abolition of the Private Owner-
ship of Land.
The Referendum.
The Abolition of Titles of so-called “nobility, ’
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter that is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started 13 years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become the representative Australian
journal, because it possesses a vitality lacked
by the countless news paper-ventures in which
Australian capitalists have unavailingly spent
large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after th
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form—printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned- with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now afflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not befen granted
by the people over whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff. .
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red coveris equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the"pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the Maori land
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul-
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

At Hamilton (Vic.), the Salvation Army
Was lately presented with a horse which, with a
red cloak on, was fetched into the barracks and
“consecrated.”
Scene : Big Isisford (Q.) shearing station.
Chinese cook calls on boss and “gives notice”
thus:—“Misse Jones, I go way. No good stay
here; evely ni’, all ni’, ‘ fifteen two, fifteen fo’,
fifteen fi’, up to Blidge you lie, you come ou’si’,
you plurry lie.’ No good, clibbage. I go. No
sleep.”
A man was recently fined, at Rochester
(Eng.), for dragging a quantity of hair out of his
wife’s head. Plaintiff appeared with the hair in
her hand. Said the defendant: “That hair she
is showing you has been in the family for genera-
tions. She brought it with her when she sum-
moned me five years ago. My father was fined
for pulling it out, and so was my grandfather.
That hair’s a dashed hairloom, yer washup.” But
jt was no use.
Dear Bulletin, — That yarn about a death-
adder suiciding at Warren, N.S.W., with its
“sting” is bushman’s bunkum, and poor stuff even
at that. The boozy, Bathurst-burr-besieged town-
ship of Warren is just the sort of place I’d expect
to hear of a death-adder with a “sting” in its
tail being found, and a man named M'Ginnis is
just the sort of person (be kroipes) that I’d expect
to make the “discovery.” They want a little
more sense up Warren way and a little less
M'Ginnis. The Warren Herald man has been
clumsily and vulgarly hoaxed. This vulgar, irra-
tional superstition regarding an Australian snake
so well-known as our death-adder (Acanthophis
antarctica) makes one feel ill, for 10 years ago
all intelligent Australians frankly admitted
that I had stamped it out. M'Ginnis’s
stupid yarn is only another instance of the
fact that concerning all questions of Australian
natural science the bushman of to-day is as
ignorant as the bushman of 60 years ago.
M'Ginnis out-Ginnises himself in imagining that
the bite —much less the “sting”—of Acanthophis
antarctica would kill itself. If one death adder
bit and injected all its poison into another it
would in no way harm it. No venomous snake
can kill itself or one of its own species with its
poison—times out of number I have proved this.
I don’t believe that the death-adder at Warren
committed suicide, and I don't believe that any
“ post-mortem examination” was held on its body.
In fact, 1 don’t believe there was any death-adder
there at all; but I do believe, firmly and con-
scientiously, that there is somewhere in N.S.W.
a place called Warren and a person named
M'Ginnis. —Yours, &c., H. J. M ‘Cooky.

At the Stage Door.
(BY PATCH[?]TT MARTIN)
Beg pardon, Miss—are you the star
We hear so much of, near and far ;
Eh ? Deg your pardon—oh, you are ?
And who am I? Well, p’raps you’ll deign
To pause and glance at me again ?
What’s that ? You think that I’m insane r
Ah no I Not even seven years’ space
Can from my memory quite efface
Your lustrous eyes and queenly grace.
'Tis strange that we should thus have met.
But not that you should thus forget
A long-since vanished amourette.
I, who was then a brilliant beau,
Might be a model now for “Woe
See, from my boot obtrudes ray toe.
While you, as in the days of old.
Have silken dresses, rings of gold,
And are so charming to behold !
Pray, don't turn pale, and wherefore start?
What though we both pledged hand and heart
You’ve played throughout a splendid part.
Scene I.—“Betrothed”—one moment, Miss,
'Twas these parched lips received the kiss
That was to herald life-long bliss.
But when the wished-for day drew near,
There came a certain wealthy peer,
Whose smile was like a satyr’s leer.
I saw him kiss you on the cheek,
And in a fit of foolish pique
Sailed for Australia that same week.
I doubt not he admired your charms,
And clasped you in his wrinkled arms,
While diamonds stilled cold Virtue’s qualms.
But meantime in Crimean shirt
I digged, to find ’midst clayey dirt,
The metal that did all the hurt.
Then for a while the curtain falls;
It rises, when on Melbourne walla
I see your name in capitals !
And so again we chance to meet
Here by the stage-door in the street;
A meeting after years—how sweet 1
But tell me all the story, pray:
Was he, then, poor as well as grey 7
Or—did his lordship run away f
Some other day, perhaps, you’ll tell;
You now await the prompter’s bell—
I recollect stage business well.
Adieu ! and should I ever chance
To write a drama or romance,
I need not filch my plot from France.

The Prohibition movement is assuming
awful proportions in Maoriland. It is no longer a
campaign against drink, but against the indivi-
dual drinker. In the smaller towns the move-
ment has an eye on everybody, and if it considers
that any person’s nose is too vividly illustrated,
or his breath is not up to sample, some reverend
person calls upon him. Then, having leaned his
black umbrella against the door post, he extracts
with his black-gloved hand a note-book from the
tail-pocket of his black coat, and asks him
what he drinks. While he is waiting for an
answer he fishes out a pencil from his black
waistcoat and stands ready to enter the name of
the drink in his book. If the information is not
satisfactory he most likely calls on the woman
next door and asks if the suspected person is ever
seen intoxicated, if anyone has noticed his breath,
if he looks wild on Saturday nights, if he comes
home late and has a difficulty with his keyhole, if
he swears or makes a disturbance, if he is ever
seen bringing home drink, and various other ques-
tions. He notes down all the answers, and
demands whether the interrogated female’s own
husband is strictly sober, and whether she has any
suspicions concerning anybody else in the neighbour-
hood. Then he goes round to the back and looks
over the fence to see if there are bottles lying
about.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required,
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province—you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director
Literary Communications to be addressed J. P.
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully rend and acknowledge in the
'Correspondence” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Terse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications ivill be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MSS
when the address is specified and stamps are enclosed.
But we desire those sending MSS. to distinctly under-
stand that we are not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of such MSS.

SATURDAY, MARCH 17, 1894.
The Social Scavenger.
Last week, his Honor Chief Justice Darley,
of New South Wales, in giving his decision
in the Tb Kloot divorce-case, took the
opportunity to make sundry trenchant
remarks concerning that social scavenger, the
private detective. The Tb Kloot case was
like many others of its kind. It was the
story of a young husband and a young wife
in comparatively humble circumstances, and
an elderly and obscene admirer of reputed
affluence and considerable social status. The
husband grew suspicious, and being unable
to satisfy himself as to the doings of his wife
and her elderly admirer, and it being
necessary, in any case, to get corroborative
evidence before the law would grant him
redress, he employed a private detective
to watch the proceedings of the pair. This
person assumed a horizontal attitude at the
back of the house, and through a gimlet hole
he saw sundry unpublishable doings which he
afterwards retailed in court. He, presum-
ably, couldn’t well help retailing them,
being sworn to tell the whole truth and
nothing but the truth, and as the Court
granted a decree nisi on his evidence
there was plainly no serious doubt as to
his veracity. Nevertheless, Judge Darley
went somewhat out of his way to load the
detective with opprobrium, and heaped on
him many abusive adjectives. According to
his Honor’s comprehensive vocabulary, as
reported in the daily press, the miserable
testifier was “unmanly,” “brutal,” “con-
temptible,” “ cowardly,” “ disgusting,”
“wicked,” and many other things, and
inferentially he was, in a general way, brother
to the pariah dog and the hyena, and first-
cousin to the earth-worm, and uncle to the
crow that feeds on the decayed bullock out
in the Never-Never.
* * #
Judge Barley’s state of mind on this
occasion is an interesting study. The evi-
dence of the garbage-hunting witness was so
strongly corroborated that its accuracy was
hardly even called in question. Therefore,
the Judge, to all intents and purposes,
abused him, not because he was necessarily
dirty by instinct, but because the truth which
he told was dirty. He was practically abusing
the truth, which is as ineffective and as
foolish as throwing stones at the solar
system. But the truth, as it happens, is
generally dirty, and Mr. Darley, who is
paid £3500 a year to listen to dirty truths
and weigh one unclean verity against another,
should, at least, be aware of this fact.
Moreover, strangely enough, all his copious
flow of language was reserved for the person
who saw certain obscene facts ; concerning
the people who did them his criticisms were
milk-and-watery by comparison. The well-
connected gentleman inside the house who
perpetrated the especially objectionable sin
escapes without one very violent adjective,
but the man of no social standing outside
the house, who was merely a witness of his
conduct, gets the full blast of his Honor’s
copious objurgation. This looks like a
serious discrepancy. If the truth is to pre-
vail, as it is supposed to do in law-courts,
then so long as there are men, either of
good social standing or otherwise, who
habitually do unlawful acts, there must
be other men whose business it is to
watch them through any available orifice,
and afterwards explain the circumstances
to a sensitive and hypercritical Judge.
This witness was blasted by a profuse and
fluent legal dignitary for discovering an
especially disgusting crime, and the only
possible inference is that he shouldn’t have
discovered it. And if he shouldn’t have dis-
covered it, then, presumably, no other man
should either, under penalty of similar
reproach. From which it naturally appears
that because the offence was disgusting it
shouldn’t have been discovered at all, and,
according to his Honor’s logic, it is only
necessary, in a well-ordered and ideal com-
munity, for an offender to be sufficiently
offensive to be secured from legal interference
altogether.
Judge Darley, while furiously denouncing
what was done in this case, strangely enough
altogether omitted to explain what ought to
have been done. This is another serious
discrepancy, and he is therefore invited to
consider the following alternatives. In the
first place, Tb Kloot might have put up
with his wife’s infidelity, and become thereby
a tame accomplice in his own dishonour. In
the second place, he might have done his
own detective business, and told the un-
wholesome tale of the elderly and well-
connected admirer himself without any
corroboration. In this case the abuse which
was levelled at the detective would probably
have been levelled at the husband instead ;
and then, having no independent evidence
and being strongly contradicted by his wife
(who, as a nurse, was, it would be argued,
colorably privileged to comport herself off-
handedly with men) and her elderly and
purely benevolent “protector,” who was also
colorably privileged by reason of his age, he
might possibly have found himself landed in
gaol for aggravated perjury. Or, if he escaped
this danger, he would assuredly have had his
faithless wife fixed on him for life, and
would probably have been gaoled for in-
ability to pay her paramour’s costs, and when
he got over this difficulty—if he ever did get
over it—he would have been gaoled again
for inability to pay maintenance money.
Or, as yet another alternative, he might
have taken the law into his own hands and
shot his wife’s seducer, whereupon he
might possibly have been sent to the
gallows, or to a long term of penal servitude.
Failing these alternatives the only avail-
able course was to set a watch on the guilty
pair, in which instance the choice lay
between a detective who would do the
work as a matter of business, and some
loathsome amateur who would do it as a
matter of pleasure and inherent brutality.
In either case the watcher could not know
what he would witness—perhaps if he had
foreseen the exceedingly unpleasant habits
of the well-connected gentleman even the
private detective would have refused the job.
But, having witnessed his habits, the oath
administered by Judge Barley’s Court com-
pelled him to explain them, and then the
cataclysm promptly followed.
• • #
The language of the Bench in this instance
would almost suggest—if such verdant inno-
cence were possible in an experienced lawyer
—that Mr. Darley imagines the detective
business to be, in the main, a clean, upright,
and soul-refining calling. If this is so, the
sooner he dismisses the idea the better. The
detective, whether private or official, is the
necessary hired spy and informer of civilisa-
tion. He is by profession a heartless liar ; a
detective who told the truth on all occasions
would be utterly valueless, for he would
detect nothing. He is a man who
looks through keyholes and windows and
peeps secretly round half-open doors at
all manner of loathsome sights, and takes
notes of loathsome language, and habitually
consorts, in the course of business, with
prostitutes, thieves, adulterers, and all the
other unpleasant scrap-ends of humanity,
and like the Serpent he has to pursue his
calling on his stomach all the days of his
life. It is only at rare intervals that he has
to describe these horrors in detail before a
fastidious Judge, but when the occasion
requires he does it with the nonchalance
born of long acquaintance with the miry by-
ways of life. But whether he is called upon
to describe them or not he has to know
them. He has to be all the insulting adjec-
tives which Judge Darley heaved at the
witness in the Tb Kloot divorce-case, and
failing that, crime would so effectually get
the upper hand of justice that Mr. Dar-
ley’s own person would presently not be safe
when he walked , the streets at noon-day.
But even if Tb Kloot’s minion was many
degrees worse than the average detective—
which he wasn’t, and quite deserved all the
language thrown at him—which he surely
didn’t, it ill became anybody in Mr. Darlby’s
position to make such comments. The Chief
Justice is the principal representative of the
law, and the law’s visible embodiments are
the hangman, who cold-bloodedly butchers
men for money, and the flogger, who cold-
bloodedly tortures men for money, and the
gaol-turnkey, and the man who humps away
the body after the execution; and, therefore,
the Judge is a purveyor of human meat for
the executioner and an official colleague of
the strangler. So, too, is his official brother,
the detective.
* ♦ *
A few other side issues of the Tb Kloot
case are equally remarkable. In his sum-
ming-up, Judge Darley almost hinted that
the plaintiff was an accomplice (innocent or
guilty) in his wife's dishonour because
“ when he received permanent employment
at £3 a week he declined to allow respondent
to have a servant, although he must have
known that one in the house would have been
an additional safeguard to his honour while he
was absent in the evenings.” Probably this
copious Judge never tried the experiment of
maintaining a family and paying a servant
on the princely salary of 60s. per week!
And, as a still more remarkable circumstance,
the jury recommended, and Mr. Darley
cordially endorsed the recommendation, that
the £2OOO damages awarded against the
elderly seducer should be settled on the
faithless wife and her child. So far as concerns
the child there is no objection to be made,
but the rest of the proposition forms an
interesting commentary on the wondrous
illogicalness of the law. Tb Kloot loses his
wife, his home, and presumably his happi-
ness, and his name is dragged through the
mire of the divorce-court, all through his
wife’s doing. Also he loses an amount of
money which, to a man in his position, is
practically ruinous, for though the Judge
went through the empty form of awarding
costs, these, The Bulletin presumes, are
costs of court only, according to the
invariable rule, and all the other ex-
penses, which form about three-fourths of
the whole bill, have to be borne by the
injured plaintiff himself. Further, if the
respectable seducer should by any chance
prove insolvent, Tb Kloot is responsible
for all his wife’s costs also, and can
be sent to gaol for inability to pay. And,
in consideration of all these things, he
gets absolutely nothing, while his faithless
wife gets £2OOO, which is equal to about
£lOO per annum—in fact, is paid £2OOO for
her faithlessness, for the money is forth-
coming solely because of these same acts of
infidelity, which were so offensive to a
refined intelligence that the miserable detec-
tive was fiercely anathematised for even
mentioning them in court. The ways of the
law are indeed mysterious.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Thin Issue.
That portion of the “Davies scandal” which
had become identified with the Mercantile
Bank’s final balance-sheet was disposed of last
week by a jury’s acquittal of the three de-
fendants— Davies, Millidge and Muntz—
charged with wilful intent to deceive the public.
It is now definitely decided, legally, that
nobody did deliberately “ cook ” the much-
debated balance-sheet in question. The sheet
was misleading, but the people responsible for
its appearance were guiltless of meaning anv
harm. Furthermore, the law has left in doubt
the question as to who was responsible, a matter
of small consequence, seeing that everything
was quite right. The decision in this case
justifies a number of other balance-sheets of
other banks that have suspended payment, or
gone utterly bung, at such times as they
were said to be beyond suspicion—which is
eminently soothing to the banking world.
As regards the Mercantile balance-sheet, there
were a few specially peculiar circumstances.
A loan of £IOO,OOO from another institution, a
loan hastily obtained to “strengthen” the cash
and coin reserve, was described as a “satis-
factory increase in deposits,” by which one
might suppose that the public were showing
their confidence in the Mercantile rather more
than usual. There was also a little matter of
obtaining £40,000 from a London bank in a wav
described by the London bank as “ doctoring
the accounts.” Twelve jurymen agreed to find
no criminality in these “test” transactions,
however, and as a legal curtain has been drawn
over the rest of the Mercantile Bank’s business,
the acquittal followed, as a matter of course.
Australia, for a long time sick of the miserable
legal points and solemn absurdities attaching
to this balance-sheet case, is glad to have
the farce terminated. Even as the flowers
of spring had nothing to do witli Ko-Ko’s mar-
riage to Katisha, so was this bald synopsis of
book balances irrelevant to the question of
Daviesian companies in general. Mention of
“ the tentacles ” was tabooed by the Judge,
because they had no bearing on the legal
honesty of this particular sheet. In short, the
case against Matthew Davies, as the public
have always understood it, was not under dis-
cussion at all. Nor did the question of
the possibility of Millidge and others
having known aught about the bad debts
for which no provision was marie, come before
the jury. 'The trial that ended last week
was strictly “ narrowed down,” and the
broad principles of honesty and fair dealing
had no chance to enter into it. The Mercantile
Bank came to grief through the investment of
its depositors’ money in rotten speculations,
but even the legal rightness or wrongness of
such investment remains unsettled. Does the
“ scandal ’’ end here ?
Dear Bulletin, —In Albert-street, Brisbane,
the other night, I heard a policeman accost a
young woman with an insulting question. She
gave him a sharp answer, and he arrest ed her.
At the court next morning it was shbwn that,
although once she bore a bad character, she
had reformed, and was leading a respectable
life. Hence her anger at the policeman’s ques-
tion. The Bench dismissed the case. As I
took an interest in the matter, being an eye-
witness, I made enquiries, and the girl stated
that while in the cell for the night an official
brutally assaulted her. Her body next day
was black and blue. It is well-known here
that girls in the lock-ups have no protection
whatever, and, from time to time, endure
frightful abuse at the hands of the police. They
say it is no use complaining to higher officials,
as they are not listened to. —Yours, R. D.

Thanks to Sam. Griffith’s backsliding, North
Queensland is rapidly becoming the happy
home of all shades of black, brown, and yellow
coolies. At Thursday Island recently the
Japanese held a meeting, with a Japanese law-
yer presiding, to settle the Japanese unem-
ployed question ; and telegraphed to Cairns and
Mackay offering the services of 60 Japanese for
plantation work, pearling being slack. At a
single Cairns Police Court trial the witnesses
included a Cingalese, who was sworn on a book
of Buddha ; a Malay, who preferred the
Koran; and several Chinese, who blew out
matches. At Geraldton, where there is a motley
population of Javanese, Chinese and Malays,
Miss Margaret Lyons, sister of the Geraldton
schoolmaster, was last month brutally outraged
and murdered. There is no doubt that the
crime was committed by some of the sugar-
plantation labourers, near whose camps she
passed, and three Javanese are in custody.
.The planters take such matters cheerfully.
“Only the scum of the sugar industry,” they
say ; “ and you can’t boil sugar without getting
scum.” Well, there were white men in N.Q.
once. The Bulletin fears the breed is degene-
rating.

At latest, there were 81 ex-New Australians
stranded at the lonesome Paraguayan town of
Villa Rica, and considering ways and means of
getting back to Old Australia. Some of these
had seceded, and the balance had been ex-
pelled. Single men who leave the settlement
receive £4 16s. ; married couples get £6 5s.
each, and £3 2s. 6d. for each child. This
amount probably represents their full share of
the common fund, or what remains of it, for
there is very little left out of £60 after paying
a passage to South America and other expenses:
but it is a most melancholy sum to be stranded
with in an unknown land. Plainly, Aus-
tralia will have to provide the funds for
bringing back such of these wanderers as desire
to return. At present the British Government is
moving in the matter, but the great majority of
Australians have very little reverence for the
British Government in days of prosperity, and
it is a gruesome commentary on Australian
national sentiment that this country should
leave its lost citizens to fall back on the
Imperial authorities in times of adversity. If
Australia allows the stranded Paraguayan
emigrants to be returned at the cost of the
British Treasury, this country will have a fair
claim to be regarded as the meanest one on
earth. Australia can’t profess nationalism and
accept charity from Britain at one and the same
time with any semblance of public decency.

Another priest’s-story. Summoned to the
death-bed of a stranger, his reverence said—“ Give
a sign, my poor man, to show that you die in the
church.” The departing opened one glazing eye,
and whispered—“ To hell with the Pope I ” Then
the blaggard died.

right and left for self-advertising purposes is
quoted as a proof of his heart-breaking luck in
not being able to “ keep up his title properly.”
MuNRO, being a teetotaller, his want of the funds
iiAoessarv to a sparkling champagne existence
is not so much pitied, but, otherwise, the
reduced circumstances of that misguided old
person excite sorrowful comment in religious
circles. It can’t be too often pointed out that
Davies and Mdnro, and the like of them,
never did much to merit wealth at any time.
That they were not clever men is certain.
As'boomers and dealers with other people’s
money, they were bad examples to legitimate
industry in their “prosperous” times. The
moral effect of their apparent success was
disastrous. They never dreamed of anything
better than an impossible suppiy of small land-
buyers, who would take over, at enormously
increased prices, the land which Davies and
MuNRO had bought on a dim, mysterious system
of bank “tick.” A miracle might have kept
the Melbourne land-boom going for years, in
which case Davies and Mdnro would have
absorbed tens of millions. They gambled to
win tens of millions, and when the game was
lost, it transpired that the public had to pay
for their fun. The fool who puts his own little
fortune on a horse, and “ goes broke ” in conse-
quence, is deserving of a public subscription if
these exploded bank-promoters deserve one
sympathetic word.
Mr. MTlwraith, of MHlwraith, M'Eagharn
and Cos., last week visited Newcastle (N.S.W.),
and, in an interview with the local press,
appears to have made some wild statements
about Australian labour and British capital.
Among other things, he stated that, while in
Kngland lately, he found that the general im-
pression about Australia was that, though a
magnificent country, it was hopelessly and
irretrievably ruined for investors by labour-
troubles. As an instance of this (to use his
>wn alleged words) he said that a powerful
English syndicate was formed a short time ago
to develops the New South Wales iron industry,
and their agent (though unable to find good
ironstone where it was reported to exist) dis-
covered in another locality immense deposits of
ore far exceeding in extent and richness any
yet discovered in Great Britain, with deposits
of coal in the immediate vicinity. But on
account of the unfavourable reports received
from Australia about the labour troubles, the
syndicators (how haughty their disregard for
their own interests !) abandoned the enterprise.
This is anew phase of Bull’s commercial
activity, and when contrasted with his present
feverish haste in snapping up Australian
securities looks like a lie of the clumsiest fabri-
cation. It appears still thinner when it is
remembered that another English syndicate is
at present negotiating for the purchase of the
whole of the coal-mines in the New South
Wales northern district (the hot-bed of labour
disturbances) with a view of amalgamating
them into one large trust.
I eui Bulletin, —“Australia for the Austra-
lians,” a splendid motto, is occasionally used as
a cloak for hysterical outbursts of simulated
virtue. For instance, in Melb., the other day,
a Chinaman was legally married to a girl with
the mother’s consent and in her presence.
Forthwith a magistrate sent the girl to tiie
Reformatory Schools. Nothing has been done
to the Chinese husband, nor has the “ parson ”
been arrested for illegally marrying. Conse-
quently, the ceremony appears to have been
legally valid. Under what law is the bride
sent to what is practically a gaol ? And if this
procedure be right and just in the case
of a Chinaman, what happens when a girl
marries a suspected burglar with a white skin ?
Two girls, On the recommendation of a Rev.
Somebody, lodged with a Japanese and his wife.
The Japanese, unfortunately, bore the Celestial
cognomen of Goon Lee. The girls worked at a
tobacco factory, and even the police were
obliged to own that their characters were good.
Presently, the guardians of the peace swooped
down upon the habitation of Goon Lee, and
hauled his fair lodgers before the Bench as
“ vagrants.” Evidence of their earning weekly
wages, and never having done anything more
heinous than board with an alien laundryman,
was blissfully waived from consideration, and
they were pitchforked into the Reformatory.
The papers duly vilified Goon Lee for no
earthly reason whatever, he being, it would
appear, one of the few really decent Chinese-
Japanese occupants of the city, and a smoky
supposition remains that something or other,
connected with aliens and Asiatics, has been
vindicated.—B.E. Melb., 3rd Feb., ’94.

In most speeches on the unemployed question
the speaker holds up “ the honest man ” as an
example of the bane 'of want of work. Even
Cardinal Moran lately referred to what he
called the claim of the “ honest, honourable
men ”to labour. But who are the honest men?
If a man wants work he has an equal claim
with all men just as, being alive, he has a
claim upon life. It is sheer cant to say that
it is only the honest men who have a claim
upon labour, for nobody can say who the
honest man is. Cardinal Moran might regard
him as the man who “attended his religious
duties ” regularly ; or he might call him any
man of unblemished reputation. If these are
the only men who have a claim upon labour,
people must not be shocked to hear men of
shady reputation lay a claim upon crime ; for,
deprived of their claim upon labour, they never-
theless have bodies and souls to be kept together
and must either accept charity or steal. Being
disqualified for labour, they are justified in
theft. Bat the law does not allow them that
privilege ; hence, if they want to keep clear of
the law, they must work. A man shows his
good intentions by wanting work. Cardinal
Moran will be praying next for all honest men
and leaving the dishonest men to the Devil, as
he is leaving him to the unemployed fiend—
which is the Devil. There must in this regard
be no distinction among the unemployed— all
men must have a claim to labour, inasmuch as
it is the unemployed question that has much to
do with men being dishonest.
In the progress of city milk from cow to con-
sumer, the cow earns, and the consumer pays,
three distinct profits. The cow, of course, gets
no profit. She has a lot of sour grass while she
lives, if her lower jaw works assiduously; and,
when she dies, a sumptuous funeral in the
stomachs of roast-veal restaurant patrons.
Nevertheless, the cow is an item in the calcula-
tion ; since, next to the creek, she is the chief
source of the milk. After the cow comes the
farmer, who robs the calf, milks the cow,
empties the creek, and sells the product to a
big milk concern for 3d. a gallon. Then
the big milk concern provides horses and
carts for which it charges 355. a week
rent, and sells the milk to a milkman for
fid. a gallon. Then the milkman advertises
immense reductions, and sells the milk to the
consumer for Is. a gallon, or 3d. a quart. Thus
the milk, in travelling from fanner to con-
sumer, is charged with 390 per cent, increase
on the farmer’s price. Distribution costs three
times as much as production ; and the middle-
man cheerfully pockets three-fourths of the
value of the milk. Some of these days a farm-
ing genius may arise to introduce in Australia
the system of Southern Europe, and drive his
own cows to be milked at the customers’ doors
morning and evening. Then the consumer will
get his supply cheaply and directly, and know
it is as pure as udders can make it; while
farmer and cow will work together to their
joint profit, instead of being jointly exploited
by the parasites of industry.
Trials of endurance, or of strength, or races,
between alcoholisers and teetotallers, vege-
tarians and caruivorarians, &c., &c., are just
about as useless, as matters of test comparison,
as would be contests between swallows and
sharks. In every contest everything depends
upon the man himself. Were it possible to get
two men of exactly the same height, breadth,
tenacity of fibre, lung-capacity, &c., &c., breed
them for (say) 20 years in the same climate and
under exactly the same conditions of physical
exercise, &c. —keeping as the only variant the
one essential to be tested, such as the exclusion
of meat or of grog —then a trial of strength or
endurance between two such impossibilities
mitjht mean something ; on the other hand, it
might not, for a sudden sprain, strain, chill,
or change of mood even, might create a
handicap to the advantage of one. The
fancy tests brought about by the various
showmen on either side are mere amusing
gambles, of no value whatever in the settlement
of the physical questions involved. The latter
can only be approximated, and the approxima-
tions can only be teased out by generalisations
of the results of experiments conducted on a
large scale. What man does not know half-a-
dozen others who can do 50 per cent, extra
work with their interiors painted with alcohol;
andauother half-a-dozen who“never feel better”
than when they “ knock it off entirely?” The
human individual varies as much in respect of
his physical powers as he does in respect of the
lines on his palms—only more so. And whisky-
drinking and meat-eating have nothing what-
ever to do with the variation, but only concern
the individual.

Brewer Bass, created Lord Burton eight years
ago in consideration of his allegiance to Glad-
stone’s Irish policy, has formally renounced the
faith of his untitled self, and joined the
Unionist party in the House of Lords. One
more argument for the abolition of the House
aforesaid. Asa rule, the men promoted by
Gladstone to “another place” don’t wait
eight years before ceasing to lick the hand that
fed them. They declare themselves in their
true characters without letting a decent time
elapse, after the manner of a widow changing
her name ere the grass grows green over Late
Lamented, Esq. And these are, perhaps, a
little less despicable than the beer magnate
whose alleged “Liberal” principles trickle
away into the stream of Toryism, instead of
rushing thither like flood waters pouring
through a broken dyke. The thing has to be
done, and it is better done quickly. A commoner
coveting a peerage is a snob beyond question.
A true snob echoes the sentiments of the
Sovereign, and the Sovereign is the symbol of
Toryism. Even as every Australian knight is
the enemy of Republican doctrines, so is every
British peer an inevitable opponent of all
schemes that make for liberty. Those dis-
honest publications, the constitutional news-
papers, have set up a bogus distinction between
Tory peers and “ Liberal ” peers, and have gone
so far in humbug as to talk about “Radical”
peers. There is but one brand of peer, and the
newer his title the more evident his brand.
Gladstone himself being merely a Tory in a
mild form, he has, on most questions, a follow-
ing among the Lords. When (if ever) a Demo-
cratic Government starts operations in England
the Peers will oppose it in a solid body, and the
mob will probably rush the Upper Chamber one
afternoon and cast the pillars of the State into
the street.

lowered that it is quite common to find able-bodied men
working on stations for 155., and even less, per week ; the
cause being, so His said, the insecurity of tenure. Surely
that is not a fair wage for any white man ! It is not as if
the squatting industry would not stand better wages. An
official declaration should he obtained from the Pas-
torabsts’ Union, that their members shall not pay a lower
wage than, say, £1 p r week in return for the extension
of their leases. If the Central Division squatters intend
to keep the present rate of pay going then I, for
one, would just as soon see anew race of men
take their places. Station-work, with the exception of
shearing, is the worst-paid of any in Australia, and the
extension of the leases is simply making a present of
big money to the lessees or their bankers. Station work
is not like most other employments. Once a man enters
that kind of life he is damned for all other work. There
is no prospect of a rise ; it is the same thing year after
year, in all weather—he has no chance of improving his
position. “ Abandon hope all ye who enter here ” should
be written large on the door of every station-hut, for
never did a sphere of toil afford so little chance of rising
from the ruck as station-life in Australia. The man who
lives in the hut knows that he will die in the hut; there is
no hope for him ; down he is and down he will remain till
the end.—Rouseabout, 20th Feb., ’94.
This is fche jubilee year of the Australian wine-
trade. In 1844, Captains Weston and Ogilvy,
who had learned something about grape treat-
ment during the Peninsular War, made a first
attempt to inspire the locals with a thirst for
pure wine from their vineyard on Parramatta-
road. The gallant captains placed a few hhds.
with Sizar Elliott, of Sydney, and he, after
bottling, labelling, and capsuling in smart
style, put the stuff on the market at 15s. a
dozen. The Gov. bought a case or two, and
sundry other official persons tried to give
the new industry a lift, but the general public
would have none of it. The wine, says Mr,
Elliott (he is still alive and hearty in Melb.),
was very good ; better than most of the grape-
juice offered for sale to-day, but people refused
to take it seriously. Had they then hailed the
vineyard idea, Australia might by this time have
become a mighty wine-growing and exporting
country, with millions of bottles of the native
article accumulating cobwebs in thousands of
private cellars, and a population proud of offer-
ing its friends a glass of good stuff —laid down
in the sixties or seventies. At present, how-
ever, Australia is a land of houses that don’t
possess cellars wherein to lay it down, and of
householders who continue to swill beer and
whisky, without regard for climatic considera-
tions, for the reason that beer and whisky are
great British institutions.
Fees to civil servants have been practically
abolished on the continent of Australia, though
there are some bad exceptions. But, as a rule,
if a man is fined, the fine goes into the revenue.
In Tasmania, it too often goes into the pocket of
the civil servant who levies the fine. The fee-
system flourishes there in unmitigated flagrancy.
The Crown-Solicitor, for example, gets his
salary, and he gets no man knows how much
more for doing precisely the work for which his
salary is paid. Scores of big and little officers
emulate the Crown-Solicitor, according to their
degree, and the public are taxed in gross to pay
them wages, and in detail to pay them fees.
Revolt has long simmered, and at last J. C.
Mitchell, a Tasmanian native, has come
forward to bell the official cat. The support
which he received was so instant and unanimous
that the public mind is evidently made up to
abolish the abuse. Premier Dobson’s feeble
extenuation should be another nail in the coffin
of his feeble Government.

Sydney Telegraph on the acquittal of Matthew
Davies and others :
The only satisfactory part in the entire history of the
case is that now the parties accused have at last had a
fair trial before a jury, where their social and political
position counted for no more than if they were ordinary
uninfluential members of the community. They have had
the benefit of the most skilful legal defence, and the case
has been conducted by one of the ablest Judges on the
Victorian bench. Beginning on February 19, and ter-
minating on March 7, the inquiry has been of the most
exhaustive character, and there is every reason to believe
that not a point on either side has been left undeveloped
by the counsel engaged in the case. The result is that
the jury find them not guilty, and upon that the accused
may be congratulated, because it acquits them absolutely
of the criminality which the public heretofore had been
tempted to affix to them. They can now honestly say
that, independent of friends at court, and despite their
social position and their political connections, they have
been able to go through the terrible ordeal with un-
blemished character.
The same paper next day ;
As the Judge pointed out, it was not a crime that the
directors knew little of the actual position of the bank ;
it was not a crime for the bank to pay an 8 per cent, divi-
dend out of profits with an apparent large surplus, when
a fortnight afterwards it stopped insolvent; it was no
crime tb borrow £IOO,OOO from another bank, with the
stipulation that it should not be re-lent, with the object
of swelling the bank’s cash; and in regard to the group
of so-called Davies companies, it was not a crime for
those companies to figure as holding extensive
amounts of uncalled capital, and to raise millions
of deposits and debentures on the strength of
that capital, when the bulk of such capital
really represented shares in the hands of a tew interested
people. It was no crime to transfer properties from one
of the group to another at ever-increasing valuations, and
to treat those increments as profits, and to declare large
dividends to those shareholders on the strength of them.
It was no crime that the public did not know how things
were managed. Each transaction received the formal
sanction of the board of directors appointed by the share-
holders, and was perfect in form and legality. Only it
would be well if practical legislation could provide against
such practices in the future, could protect the public from
such bogus profits, and could make directors answerable
to depositors, debenture holders, and shareholders for the
correctness of the statements made to them.

PROSPECTING.
There is some splendid country
Where slate and quartz abound
The ranges on the Nicholson I mean
I’ve fossicked every gully
And every hill around
But not a blooming colour have I seen.
I dollied stone from every reef
I worked and toiled in vain
There’s Gold there yet thats my Belief
But I am Hanged if I try it again.
My prospects there were none to bright
The whole place looked so s; d
With not a human being in sight
It nearly drove me mad.
I rode about with dish and bag
Likewise with pick and shovel
Untill my poor unfortunate nag
At times could scarcely travel.
So Bye Bye to the ranges
They’ll see this kid no more
I am going to try ray luck
Out further West
That was My Intention to
Go out there Before
But I thought this Barren
Country worth a test.

March 14, 1894.
My Dear Moorabinda—
Paraguay Lane can’t be a clever Moses or he
wouldn’t have ukased, during the maiden voyage
of the “Royal Tar,” that no woman should show
herself on deck after sunset. Of course nobody
took any notice of the ridiculous order, and Mr.
Lane, it is said, sulked in his cabin for the rest of
the trip. Yet the Lane programme was full of
sentiment and every Jack was to have his Jill.
Heaven watches over newspaper interests : the
burglar burgles mostly in the silly season. A
Sydney daily gratefully put into its account of a
recent suburban crow-bar episode a sketch of the
lady’s bedroom almost as it appeared on the
eventful evening, but not quite. There was the
virtuous couch certainly, but the heroine had
been carefully lifted out of it —probably by Mrs.
Grundy, The idea conveyed was that Bill Sikes
had bagged the lady along with the spoons and
forks.
Nobody has ever invented a good burglar-trap*
A well -to- do Sydney man whose assets
weigh on his mind o’ nights, always leaves an
abundance of “ doctored ” whisky on his dining-
table ere going to bed—just as the housewife puts
strong cheese into the mouse-trap. I fear that
will never save his silver, for the real burglar is
almost necessarily a teetotaller. Calculating
criminals can’t afford to get drunk; cheerful
potations are the privilege of honest folk. Witness
the teetotal saints and their land-jobs.
Some original people entertain their burglars.
Not long ago an Edgecliffe-road resident found
two strange men in his dining-room at 1 a.m.
“My good fellows,” he said coolly, “there’s
really nothing in the house worth bagging. Sup-
pose you stop and take a little supper with me in-
stead of invoicing the lumber. I promise to say
nothing of this visit.” The astonished house-
breakers were too surprised to do more than sit
meekly down to table while their host skirmished
for forage, ultimately discovering bread and
butter and a tin of tongues. The host’s efforts
to open the can were so hopeless that one of the
burglars arose and, witha polite “ Allow me,’’and
a professional turn of the wrist, whisked off the
top twinkling. The scene didn’t end with a
rush of constabulary. The visitors ate and went,
and tfie host held a candle for them as far as the
gate. There never is any thrilling denouement in
the true story.
One never knows how to take a woman. For
instance, I would never have anticipated that her
Worship Mayor Yates, of Onehunga (Maoriland),
would seek to introduce the cloture in her little
talking-shop, limiting orators to smin. for speak-
ing to a motion and 3min. for a reply. And yet
it’s a womanly-enough action when you come to
look into it. The feminine tongue is certainly
hung in the middle, and the sound of it is sweet
music to the owner, at least—but, then, I never
heard it said that women were fond of hearing
other people talk.
A Maoriland man writes me in a burlesquey
spirit to say that the women voters over there
gave an amusing performance on polling-day. It
was a novelty to be bailed up on the pavement by
decent women (the decency, I suppose, was the
novelty), most of them young and pretty into the
bargain, and recalling one’s bazaar-experiences.
The pretty women were diplomatically sent on to
the kerbstone to coax indifferent men into plump-
ing for Sftiith, while the back - number she-
suffragists were carefully shut up in committee-
rooms playing with red tape and blue pencils until
the poll was declared. My correspondent does
not say that picked companies of nice young men
with lovely moustaches and London Journal legs
were out in search of the women’s votes.
Regarded in a strictly fashionable light, Equal
Suffrage is a burst bubble all over Australia. In
Sydney one ladyship withdrew her countenance
from it for (many people thought) a merely
querulous reason, disguising the real one ; and
now in Brisbane a “society” lady washes her
hands of it altogether, because she objects to the
ridiculous notion of one-woman-one-vote—making
Jill as good as her “missis.”
Mind you, I don’t say that the Women’s Rights
movement may not actually benefit by becoming
a pariah among the fashionable fads of the day—
the only wonder is that it ever got a stylish
clientele considering that no Governor’s wife ever
officially recognised it. But smart people in
search of a young and healthy fad are cautioned
to steer clear of the Women’s Rights notion as
they would shy at the bottomless pit. From a
daring sort of parlour pastime Equal Suffrage has
got down to be a subject of heated discussion in
the kitchen, and though genuine partisans may
see more hope for the “ cause ” than ever, the
perfumed humbugs who originated the idea wish
they could exorcise the whole business. After all,
the society woman isn’t politically fit for much
higher things than Primrose-Leaguing, or to pass
a bill for the encouragement of fore-lock pulling
among the virtuous peasantry.
Lady Duff has sent out invitations for an Easter
ball at Govrnentouse, on 29th inst.—also for a
garden-party—to follow the ball, of course. The
sequence I want to see is a ball following a garden-
party, and then the visitors on the grass would
be quite sure they weren’t asked to simply make a
clean sweep of sandwiches. At one vice-regal
garden-party coming on the heels of a ball some-
body found a hairpin in a glass of jelly.
The first charity ball of the season will be the
brightly-promising dance to be held under vice-
regal patronage on 30th inst., in Paddington Town
Hall, in aid of the Orphanage of Mercy. Tickets
(65.) are obtainable from Mrs. Hotham, “Lans-
down,” Edgecliffe-road, Woollahra.
Apropos of nothing in particular, they are say-
ing down South that when you are pitchforked
into the high places of sassiety and your humble
relative departs this world, you prove yourself not
worth your society salt if you commit the error of
going into mourning for him or her. It leads to
such awful commiserations if yon do. “ Did I
ever meet your poor dear grandpa in sassiety, Lady
Snuffkins ?” says the sympathetic visitor. Where-
upon Lady Snuffkins says “No,” faintly, behind
a black-bordered handkerchief, and waits, in cold
horror, for the next question, which is generally
more to the point. All this is obviated by simply
begging the question when an unpresentable
relative “pegs out.” There is one Baratarian
mushroom “noble” who evidently follows this
golden rule, or else he began life as an only
orphan. Nobody ever saw that family turn out in
an inky cloak, good mother.
New fashions in manners land in Australia
from every mail-boat, and the very latest phase of
Anglophobia renders the patient unintelligible to
some plain people. You must have been ’Ome,
you know, to do it properly—that is, allude to
London airily as “town.” “I saw Pagliacci in
town,” is the very latest way of saying that you
sampled Leoncavallo in London. No person who
turns his trousers up round the hems on a dry
day and carries a shepherd’s crook to support his
tottering footsteps ever makes crude geographical
allusions to “ London ” nowadays—not in Aus-
tralia, anyhow.
Speaking of “town”—when you are taking a
flight to that classic village don’t, in a burst of
generosity, give your parrot or your poodle-dog
to your bosom friend as a keepsake—but rather
board out the blue monkey, or whatever the
animal is, until you return. For instance, a
Sydney society girl went ’Ome a while ago and
ere taking ship she made an impressive present
of her Skye-terrier to a gentleman friend. But
when she returned the other day the first thing
she did was to hunt up the man with the dog.
“ Oh, Mr. Blank, you’ll give me my doggie back,
won’t you ? They’re so fashionable in town !”
People who accept pets from vanishing ladies
and let themselves get fond of them—l mean the
pets—are advised to ponder over this item.
The attitude —I had almost written pose—of
N.S.W. Attorney-General Heydon over the dual-
bathing question reminds me of a nervous bather
getting into the water gingerly on a cold day. The
Bill allows bathing along the beaches, yet men,
women, and children mustn’t go all in together
like a shoal of herrings, but separately on the
familiar “ladies to the left’’plan—only that under
the new regime the barriers will be invisible, and
Miss Aphrodite will have to get used to critical
male spectators sizing her up along the beach.
And, after all, the invisible-barrier idea is a far
more discreet plan than the style of erecting a
groggy fence patched with jam-tins round the
ladies’ reserve, and encouraging—by mysteriously-
worded notice-boards —stray Paul Prys to seek
for cracks in the palings while Venus underneath
imagines herself almost as secure as in her own
bathroom by reason of the ferocious sign-boards
and doddering palings hereinbefore alluded to.
The men, it seems, are to bathe alone, and the
ladies are to have the care of the small fry. But
I don’t think children should be allowed to bathe
with their aged grandmothers, and so on—it
engenders that familiarity which doesn’t lead to
respect. A possible little Johnny may be only six,
but the shock that seizes him when he sees ma
take off her curly hair and hang it on a geebung
tree-spray and hide her shop teet'h in a crab-hole
preparatory to ducking herself, may make a cynic
of him ten years ahead.
From “neck to knee” is the latitude of the
N.S.W. bathing-dress according to Act of Parlia-
ment. (In my mind’s eye, Horatio, do I see, in a
dim, religious light, intensely “proper” Attorney-
General Heydon, cutting out the paper patterns
at Parliament House). But why women should
be obliged to carry more topsail when they’re
dipping than when they’re dancing or theatre-
going is difficult to understand, and I think this Lord
Chamberlain-ish style of regulating our bathing-
suits is distinctly against the Constitution, as
being an implied intimation to Victoria Regina
that her ideas of decency don’t correspond with
Heydonian modesty. The decollette corsage of
to-day is encouraged by Queen Victoria, and it is
probably only that fact which keeps our discreet
A.-G. from ukasing that from ear to heel is full
ball-dress in decorous N.S.W. However, royalty
doesn’t hold drawing-rooms on the sea, and there
is no marine precedent to stop the amateur censor
in his present gallop.
The Royal Agricultural Society holds a specially
magnificent Easter shivoo at Moore Park from
the 21st to the 27th inst. The grounds are to be
illuminated by electricity every evening and the
attention of “country visitors” is especially
directed to this fact, probably lest the agriculturist
from Cow Flat should attempt to light his pipe at
the arc lamp and fall back a corpse into the bosom
of his family. There is to be the usual great show
of horses and miscellaneous country produce, and
—as something specially appropriate to the season
of the indigestible hot-cross bun —fireworks are to
be laid on for three evenings.
I hear that the secretary of Sydney, Women’s
Hospital collection committee, the other day,
called on Lady Duff, with a mild request that she
would preside over a table set at a street corner
on Saturday mornings, for the purpose of receiving
contributions. Her ladyship was afraid she would
not be able to do so. Then ivould she, could she,
allow the MisSes Duff to take a table—of course,
with a chaperone ? No, they were too young.
As a last inducement it was urged—“ They do it
at ’Ome.” Even this failed, and the committee is
—well, the committee are annoyed.
Yours faithfully,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Melbourne Confidences.
March 13, 1894.
The Australian Cup gathering at Flemington,
though socially select, was not at all richly uphol-
stered, many of the best-bred gowns showing
signs of a season’s wash and wear. But this last
spurt of summer, coming in the middle of an
autumn meeting, had upset calculations.
Lady Hopetoun was brilliant in pink faille, the
bodice fichu’d with ficelle lace. The Countess,
who of yore appeared to find agloomy satisfaction
in being outshone by local birds of Paradise, now
out-Herods Herod by her gay colours. Strolling
on the lawn last Tuesday she resembled a full-
blown rose.
The vice-regal tresses were not arranged in the
all-pervading “bun,” but in small tight rolls.
Rather a mistake, I think. No woman, whatever
her position, should submit to looking “ scrimped ”
about the head when pads are obtainable, and
other females, with perhaps not half the hair, are
making quite a phenomenal show.
The Governor looked laboriously affable in his
favourite grey coat —that monotonous garment
which is said to be shipped out to him in
consignments of a dozen at a time, and which
doesn’t suit him a bit. His pale, chemist-
like complexion cries aloud for a dark-blue
contrast. One could forgive him if he emu-
lated the typical impresario and sported a
fur collar. Considering that the hardy Earl has
been boiling a refractory billy under the broad,
blazing canopy of Heaven, it may seem strange to
the uninitiated that he has not acquired a little
sunburn or a casual freckle. I have it as a State
secret that the consumption of vaseline and
kalydor at Guv’ment House is not confined to
“ my lady’s ” dressing-room.
Miss Chirnside, in pink and black floral silk
and an artless ribbon sash, wore one of the rose-
trimmed “Gardener’s daughter” hats that raged
at the beginning of the season and are now being
thrown off cheap at the clearing sales. The
fragile Queenslander, Mrs. Steve Fairbairn, was
girlish as ever in a simple gown. Hubby, one of
the few black-coated men visible, towered a head
and shoulders above the little lady.
Heliotrope-muslined Miss Bon Watson, heroine
of the latest engagement, seemed to be enjoying
plenty of vice-regal patronage.
In spite of recent visitations to country houses,
the Hopetouns don’t get more gushing as their
departure draws near ; indeed they seem to keep
even their quondam hosts at a respectful distance.
Though our chief personage gets credit for being
geniality personified, he can hold his own with the
Countess at averting familiar handshakes from
belles who are not used to finding themselves kept
at bay. They both looked bored on Tuesday, and
the only time they smiled to any great extent was
when they escaped and got clean away by a
strategic movement. An individual who started
in pursuit received a haughty British snub for his
pains. But who can wonder that the suite
palls on their Nibses at times? Some of its
members don’t impress one as exciting cage-
fellows.
A Toorak magnate, erstwhile of mining fame,
paraded the lawn with a guinea-ed stamp, his
festive appearance in marked contrast to the garb
of his bachelor days. The sharer of his millions
sported an up-to-date “ basqued ” marcella, which
missed its chiefest charm through the jacket-tail
having been sat on and curled up at the edges.
“ How to be happy though rich ” seems to be as
knotty a problem as its antithesis. The wealthiest
girl at Flemington tramped the Lawn the whole
afternoon with a juvenile relative as drearily as a
shepherded boarding-school Miss, while an impe-
cunious youth, who won <£s on the hurdle-race,
made the stand ring with his shouts of jubilation.
Ho slapped his legs in ecstatic applause, until one
shuddered at the thought of his bruises. Do men
bruise ? The subject invites a newspaper corres-
pondence from married ladies,
A society matron was well to the fore in white,
with unruly shoulder-capes, which persisted in
blowing up, and had to be re-adjusted continually
by one or other of her numerous escorts. The
study of cape adjustment is certainly interesting.
Mrs. Septimus Miller, in a chiffoney confection
of glowing petunia, reminded one rather of a paper
valentine ; and Mrs. Robert Chirnside, in soft
green and brown, was several degrees more
effective.
Very short skirts are supposed to be fashionable,
bub, at the races, ankles were made a feature of by
only one woman of ton. Her extremities were
kept in countenance by that distinguished Oriental
visitor, Mr. Meennacohayle, whose small feet,
which hardly looked equal to balancing his rajah-
like proportions, must have aroused the envy of
many a long-heeled johnnie.
Some of our most exalted race-going families are
doing a period of deep mourning, so the autumn
meeting saw them nob. The whole Clarke con-
nection were absent, of course, as were, also, the
Manifolds. I hear that Miss Nellie Manifold,
whose wedding was recently postponed owing to
her mother’s death, is not going to stave off the
ceremony for long. Possibly she is anxious to
make an early start at improving her English-
man s horsemanship, in which branch of education
he is sadly deficient. Not that Romeo mounts on
the wrong side, or tumbles off as soon as the old
mare starts _ a trot, bub he can’t keep up to his
bride-elect in the hunting-field, and whenever
hard riding is the order of the day his expression
becomes pensive.
At the Town Hall the other night, the “ Try
Excelsior” boys wore harangued and presided
over by Chief Justice Madden. Our newest, or
nearly newest, knight, enthroned on a springy
chair and encompassed about by the weaker sex,
might easily have fancied himself a pasha in the
midst of his harem. When he mopped his brow
with his handkerchief an awful idea that
he would presently throw the handkerchief
to somebody, with an Oriental wink, flashed
across one’s mind. Mrs. Madden, in white
and green, surmounted by a pink and green
bonnet, looked to have been born a couple of years
earlier than daughter Daisy, who carried a less
youthful melange of black and green. At the
conclusion of a trying show the Maddens were
called for by a strange, dark vehicle, mistaken by
some of the onlookers for a hearse come to the
wrong house. It must have struck a chill to the
hearts of its owners, for they elected to walk
down the street, leaving the funeral car to follow.
At “Robertson and Moffat’s”sports, last week,
I saw some of the prettiest and best-dressed girls
in Melbourne. A feature of the sports was the
alarming collapse of the competitors as soon as
they reached the winning-post. Runners tumbled
to the ground in an apparently-dying condition.
Then their friends rolled and rubbed and mas-
saged them in so wholesale a manner that, as I
heard an elderly virgin remark, “it would have
brought a blush to a cheek of leather.”
Max O’Rell, who raves about the beauty of
London drapery “ sales-ladh s,” might have found
a similar article in the same line of business here
if he had looked for it at Saturday’s soft-goods
festival. The very sweetest visions came from
behind a counter, judging by the out of the
effusive young men who guarded them, and by the
interest the damsels displayed in the agility of the
champion shopwalker.
Reverting to the question of women and
whiskers. Dr. Clara Stone, the second of our
qualified doctoresses, was waited upon not long
ago by a would-be patient who besought her, im-
plored her, to reduce his growth of whiskers by
some electric process. The poor fellow was about
to trip “ home,’’ and may have been meditating
the capture of a wealthy bride en route. Whether
or no, he wanted Dr. Clara to probe his manly
cheek with a needle and restore a portion of the
bushy country to its original state of smoothness.
Shaving wouldn’t do, it seemed. The more he
shaved the bluer his face got, and as a last re-
source he had been advised to try electrical punc-
tuation. His reason for singling out a lady to
take his hair off was not quite evident, but one
supposes that he thought she would stab him
gently. However, Miss S. refused to undertake
the contract, and the patient reluctantly withdrew.
In the wilds of Beaconsfield the florid taste of
L. L. Smith, M.L.A., gets the necessary scope.
Mr. Smith (he was observed of all observers at
Flemington) has built himself a breezy little resi-
dence, with a model poultry farm attached, away
on the Beaconsfield heights. The hen-and-rooster
arrangements are said to be highly instructive,
the garden surrounding the “chateau” is cer-
tainly a wonderful evidence of man’s inhumanity
to Nature. I have seen this garden from near and
afar. Every here and there a marble statuette
adorns a plaster pedestal, but the glory of the
pleasure grounds is chiefly upheld by a regi-
ment of thin white posts supporting glass
balls of various sizes and colours. Some of them
are twice as large as an ordinary gold-fish bowl,
and some are about the size of cocoanuts. A long
row of these smaller ornaments, standing at equal
distances apart down the side of a gravel walk,
appears to have been stuck there for the amuse-
ment of visitors who feel inclined for “ three shies
a penny.” The general effect beggars description.
It is as though the festive Smith had discovered a
giant Christmas-tree in the neighbouring forest
and carried off those “prizes” which at juvenile
parties are always left hanging on the top boughs.
There are at least a hundred glass balls glistening
in the sun. They glitter on spikes over the roof
of a rural tea-house, and I think I noticed one on
the lofty summit of the ancestral flagstaff. The
explanation of this barbaric splendour seems to be
the fact that Smith p£re was once proprietor of
Cremorne Gardens, where his famous son acquired
an early reverence for bits of colour.

Miss Jennie Lee and her weeping waif
“Jo” are filling in an odd week at Sydney Lyceum
with more success than might have been expected,
taking several things into consideration. This is
the lady’s farewell season prior to her departure
for England, and The Bulletin’s grief at the loss
of a favourite actress is tempered somewhat by
the fact that this country has already had too
much “Jo.” “Jo” has grown to be a very, very
solemn thing in these later years. He has been
“moved on” in nearly every theatre in Aus-
tralasia, and there is hardly a veteran actor
of any note in these provinces who hasn’t moved
him on at some time or other. “Jo ” has
been found weeping on every property doorstep
in the continent; and several million half-crowns
have been laid beside his dying bed by Snagsby,
the sympathetic law-stationer ; and his broom and
his death-cough are better known even than
Parkes, and several hundred tiipes more respected.
“Jo ” is about the oldest thing in Australia now,
barring the gallows, and the manner in which
Jennie Lee keeps the aged drama afloat in all its
pathos is nothing short of marvellous. Still, as
already mentioned, Australia has had almost
enough “ Jo.” Next week will be filled in with
“ The Grasshopper.”
* * * -*
“Hans, the Boatman” is to bo staged
next week at Sydney Criterion, with Miss Hen-
rietta Watson in the part of Hans’ wife—the
character so ably filled by Myra Kemble on the
last visit of the quaint Dutch- Vankee fisherman.
The career of “Captain Fritz” might not have
been so short if that musical comedy-drama hadn’t
been quite so long. “Hans” has at least the
advantage of being more condensed, and ho
appears in a new edition, whatever that may
mean. Also Miss Watson will be a tower of
strength to the show, and is, in all probability, the
actress of all others in Australia who is calculated
to give the sketchy production a lift just where it
is most needed.

No change is yet announced at Sydney
Her Majesty’s, where the Irish drama still pur-
sues its path of crime, and the Scotch father is
thrown over the cliff nightly with much empresse-
mcnt. This latter substance being French is
naturally the thing that a wicked Scotchman
would be thrown over a cliff with.
** * *
Sydney Gaiety, which has been closed so
long that few of the present generation can remem-
ber when it was open, will make a fresh start
Easter Saturday with a comedy co., under the
management of Mr. W. J. Wilson. The old house
has been dusted and otherwise freshened up, also
the old name has been dropped out of sight, and it
is now to be known as the Bijou.
** * *
The variety co. at Sydney Royal has been
strengthened by the addition of a minstrel who
wears the portentous name of Clarence Lyndal,
and does his best to live up to that weighty cog-
nomen. By the way, there is understood to be a
little unpleasantness at the Royal. “Unpleasant-
ness ” is a term which covers anything, from cross
looks to general assault.
*« * *
Fillis’ Circus has returned to Sydney after
many wanderings, and opens next Friday night
on the accustomed stand opposite Redfern Rail-
way-station. A steeplechase, vaguely designated
“The Stag Hunt,” is one of the principal new
features, and is expected to appeal strongly to the
feelings of a country where people don’t hunt
stag. There is a lamentable want of originality
about this idea, somehow. If Fillis fully under-
stood the public feeling he would change the stag-
hunt into a wild race after a defaulting banker
who would have lOsec. start and would be recog-
nisable by his bell-topper and the Bible sticking
conspicuously out of his coat-pocket.
** » »
A successful racing day accounted for an
extra over-crowd at Melbourne Princess’s last
Saturday. The wide stretch of standing room in
the dress-circle bristled with famous personages,
from Tom Hales down to members of Parlia-
ment, and the hot air was delicately perfumed by
the breath of those who had been drinking Titan’s
health. Notwithstanding a general tendency to
take things kindly, “La Mascotte ” gave only
moderate pleasure to all. This is never a fast-run
opera. Although on Saturday the pace was
furious for the first mile, it slackened off after-
wards in accordance with precedent, and the
demonstration that greeted the weighing-in was
not very hearty. The characters have no stamina
to speak of. They dash away at the outset in a
taking manner, and presently they wobble all over
the track, running wide when they oug'ht to hug
the rails and sometimes doing their best to bore
the audience. The artists in charge of these
erratic contestants are not to be greatly blamed,
Pippo, for instance, is a wayward animal showing
a good deal of temper at the finish, and Ryley
handles him as well as can be expected. A
shepherd who jibs at getting married to Nellie
Stewart is a nasty creature to manage. Bettina,
for her part, prepares to throw herself away upon
a doddering old prince, whilst Fiametta, after
lathering a tame dude with a riding whip until
they reach the distance, eventually abases herself
before his glass eye and implores forgiveness for
her previous good taste. The sporting metaphor
seems to be getting mixed.
* a *
Miss Nellie Stewart, despite some forced
vivacity in the first act, makes a charming Mas-
cotte, her share of the “Glou-Glou ” duet—which
is otherwise well sung by the beauteous Ryley—
winning more than half of a double encore. Miss
Yarley acts the part of Fiametta better than any
she has played up to date, but Tapley takes an
unduly serious view of Frettelini, her glass-eyed
suitor. Lauri, somehow, is not here a riotously droll
potentate. He appears to suffer from a sense of
royal dignity most of the time, and when called
upon to play the fool without reserve you can
almost see him blushing for his own want of pur-
pose. Courtneidge, on the other hand, makes
farmer Rocco a much brighter portion of the opera
than that hungry agriculturist has been in the
past. The part suits him, and he revels in it.
Seeing that “Mascotte” is comparatively fresh
again, and that the present cast gives new inter-
est, it will probably pack the Princess’ for two
weeks. After which, a revival of “Paul Jones,”
with Ryley in Marian Burton’s dainty shoes.
& ft m #
Maggie Moore, at the Royal, doesn’t loosen
her grip of an admiring public. One Saturday
she set up “Forty-Nine” and made that ram-
shackle fiction hum again. Maggie, as the red-
headed heiress who picked .up a musical education
on the boundless prairie, is a whole Californian
drama in herself, and the other members of the
company give valuable support to the scenery.
The coon banquet, which was always a strong
feature of “Forty-Nine,” is more gamey than
ever, but that evil-smelling bird must return to
the Royal larder forthwith. Next Saturday they
revive “The Colleen Bawn” out of respect to
St. Patrick, a gintleman vaguely understood to be
Maggie Moore’s godfather.
** * «
The same respectable saint will have to
answer in the hereafter for a production of “ The
Shaughraun ” at the Alexandra. This further
outburst of Irish sentiment is also billed for
March 17, a wake of exceptional solemnity being
now in active preparation. Meanwhile, Dan
Barry and his devoted followers are doing all they
know to discover the plot of “ Right’s Right.”
The abduction of one or two infants in the first
act has something to do with the horrors that
come after. Yet, it is probable that the show
might be shoved along to its furions climax with-
out a first act. What they chiefly depend upon
m this instance is the last act, although the
dramatic effect of a young woman dangling from
a meat-hook over a moonlit stream goes far to en-
sure a satisfactory attendance.
** » »
The Alhambra has added “a great
English comedian ” to its stock variety company.
The newcomer, Harry Steele, turns out to be an old,
old friend from London’s beery ’alls, who rambles
to the front upon roller skates, singing in a major
key, “I stand before you ! ” Whereupon he falls
with a sickening thud. After many repetitions of
this statement, and the same number of sudden
descents, the gentleman begins to lose some of his
early charm, but the performance shows original
points, nor has Time impaired its agility.

Dan barry is very Australian, and his language amongst

recently—“l do like to hear Dan swearing. It doesn't
Bound liKe swearing a bit—it sounds to me just like
poetry /”
Prof. II -rkomer is painting the portrait of Letty Lind,
which will probably ba a sensation of next Academy.
His name is not pronounced like a sneeze—“ Katchedo,”
but is “Kaithado.” The letter “c” is always sounded
in Spanish like “th” before soft vowels; as witness
Mercedes, which is “ MairtAadea ’’ —not “ Merseeds,” as
many footlighters imagine.
