The solution of the present trouble is, ot course, for the
Williamsons to play together in “ Struck Oil." What
houses there would be ! It would be like the Boucicaults’
appearance, in the “ Shaughraun," at Drury Lane. In
all good feeling, Tub Bulletin puts the suggestion to
James and Maggie and their friends. There is a tide, &c.
Nellie Stewart does her duty by her parts as con-
scientiously as ever, or more so, if that be possible. If
she over-acts them in some cases, she is none the less a
bright example to other lady artists who seem chiefly
anxious to catch the eyes of their particular friends in
front. Nellie reminds one of the bright fixed star, which
beams with untiring industry whilst the little stars try to
excite attention by winking.
The police stopped a Sunday balloon ascent at Charters
Towers (Q.), recently, on the ground that a balloon was a
gambling implement under section 11 of the Town Lands
Act!
Snazelle, the humourist, writes from South Africa:—
“ Compared to Australia, this is a brutal country to be in,
and Johannesburg is a hell. I have never spent such an
unhappy fortnight since I have been in the profession.
Pray advise everybody to keep away. There is not only
no money to be made for a company, but the records in
the cemetery of actors’ and actresses’ graves tell their own
tales.” But one never notices actors’ graves in a country
which fills one’s pockets.
Florence Young, while out rowing in Sydney harbour
lately with her sister, was pursued by a monster shark.
Everyone will sympathise with that shark.
Editor Bulletin. Sir,—ln your paper of 7th Oct., ’93
(p. 20), a local correspondent furnishes the public with
crude and unrefined misstatements re some of my dis-
coveries regarding “ curly hair.” If that paltry commu-
nication had been fully truthful I would' pass it
unchallenged. Neither in public nor in private have I
ever used such language as “ nigger temperament,”
which the vulgar correspondent unwisely attributes to
me, without even the shadow of common politeness.
Never, until reading your correspondent’s calumniatory
lines, had I thought of such a fallacy as negro tem-
perament (“ nigger temperament ”). Hence, in attribut-
ing to me that vulgar phrase, consists his dishonesty.
The gist of my knowledge regarding curly hair is embodied
in my book, entitled “ Physiognomy Illustrated,” pages
857 to 363 inclusive— ibid, page 403. Copies of that book
are in every large public library in Australia, and also in
many of the libraries of the other English-speaking
peoples. My ideas are not given to the public in the
“ blunt terms” attributed to me by that prevaricating
correspondent. Last, if not least, that illiterate inter-
nuncio is too cowardly to sign a name.—Yours, J. Shims,
M.D. San Francisco, 10th Feb., 1894.
Arthur Lawrence, once well and favourably known in
Australia, made his first American appearance, a few
weeks ago, as Parson Manders, in Ibsen’s “Ghosts.”
A Yankee paper announces the advent ot Miss Della
Fox, a new comic opera artiste: “ I think Miss Fox
should succeed. Her hair is the colour of a new clothes-
line, her limbs are as plump as autumnal sausages, and
she can bleat a comic song like a lamb calling for its
breakfast. She has all the attributes of success, and I
guess she is a good investment for managers.”
Fitzgerald Bios’, circus is in Tasmania, and after a
rapid run through that sleepy isle it goes on to Maori-
land. The co. includes the Ridg way Family (the fascinat-
ing Nellie being a leading attraction), the pony Commo-
dore, two new lion cubs and the rest of the menagerie.
Tommy, the pony, the two horses purchased from Sarah
Bernhardt when that shadowy genius sold out for the
several dozenth time, and many other features.
Richard Perristan, a one-time actor, died last month, a
beggar, in San Francisco. The one great circumstance in
Perristan’s life was his stupendous good fortune in the
Havannah lottery, where he drew a prize of £lOO,OOO, in
'75. Apparently, however, there was blood on the big
prize, and Perristan never had a vestige of luck there-
after. iiHe couldn’t even sit down on a chair like an
ordinary man without the bottom falling out of it and
dropping him through, and the last 20 years of his life are
an eloquent sermon on the curse of gambling.
Mr. Harry Rickards’ plea that he doesn’t charge for
admission to his Sunday concerts, but only takes up a
collection at the doors, will not stand in law’. The
ultimate legal view w T as fairly indicated in Victorian
Premier Patterson’s declaration that the collection at a
Sunday concert must be taken, not at the door, but
amongst the audience when it is seated.

appear nt the Opera House, Chicago, 10th May.
Dear Bulletin,— The P.P. par. in last issue about
“ astronomy Proctor ” and myself is, doubtless, very good
reading—as anything about two such celebrities must be
—but the truth is not in it. When that brilliant exponent
of “ Other Worlds than Ours ” Visited Australia 1 was a
poor young man, then unmatriculated even, and quite
uninitiated into the wily ways of managers. Thus the
whole story is as imaginary as the existence of Utopia
(Limited).—Yours, Carlyle Smytiib.

Henry Pettitt’s estate has been sworn atpearly £50,000.
He left three-fifths of his fortune to his wife and her
children, and two-fifths to the irregular Mrs. Pettitt and
her brood, a gift of £6OOO to the latter being brought into
the pool. Henry had the decency to go home to
die, almost his last wish being that he should be brought
to his wife’s house to give up his last breath.
Sydney Grundy’s “ Moonlight Club ” in “The Old Jew”
is, the Referee hints, intended for the Savage. Sydney
many years ago was proposed as a Savage, but a gentle
hint was given to his friends, and his name was with-
drawn. Hinc, &c.
Olga Nethersole has made the success ot her life at the
little London Court Theatre, where she has produced a
play called “ The Transgressor.” There is the story of a
lunatic wife, love and bigamy, with the usual heroic
determination of the wronged woman to stick to her bar-
gain, “because he loves me so.” It is said that she is
backed “by a powerful syndicate.” They always are.
Lawrence Oautley, says The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, had been engaged to play a part in the “ Gaiety
Girl," but after three or four nights he somehow dropped
out of the bill. His complimentary benefit at the Lyric
was very successful, and was a smart affair in its way.
The house was full, and no end of w,»Il dressed women
were there. Mrs. “ Myee Sydney ” Oautley is a favourite
amongst the many friends she has made in England. The
programme was a long one, and was negotiated success-
fully until it came to the turn of Lawrence and “Miss
Myee Sydney ” to play the balcony scene in “ Romeo and
Juliet.” Whether it was that Romeo was too much
occupied in rolling those eyes of his about I can’t say, but
Lawrence forgot all that poor Shakespeare had put in the
mouth of the love-sick youth, and it took the loud-voiced
prompter all his time to keep him on the track. No
doubt this did not serve to give poor Juliet much confi-
dence, and she certainly did not shine. Probably she
may do better another time, but she is very amateurish,
and all the most, friendly critic could find to say was
“ that she gave promise of doing better things by and
bye.”
The revival of “Caste,” at the Garrick, was not a
blazing triumph, says Tub Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent ; indeed, to tell the truth, it was a poor affair all
round, the bright exception being the Esther Eccles of
Miss Kate Rorke, which will take rank with any previous
performance of the part. She was charming and sym-
pathetic enough to have pleased even Robertson himself.
The George D’Alroy of Forbes Robertson was also good,
and Miss Rose Leclerq was an ideal Marquise. But what
shall I say of the rest of the crew ? Place aux dames I
Miss May Harvey was distinctly out of her element as
Polly. Abingdon, as Hawtree, apparently could not
forget that he has been for many years an Adelphi
villain. Poor young Hare is more to be pitied than
blamed for about the weakest delineation of Sam Gerridge
that I have ever seen. He was overweighted by the part,
and handicapped by the recollection of his father’s con-
ception of the character. But to me the greatest dis-
appointment of all was G. W. Anson. I had expected to
see something out of the way after hearing that he had
played old Eccles a hundred times in Australia. Did he?
well, if so, all I can say is that Australian playgoers are
very easily satisfied. He was boisterous in manner and
wanting in spontaneity of humour. The old man we
know was not a loveable being, but Anson need not have
made him such a hopelessly degraded sot. It is no-doubt
hard to steer clear of the exaggeration which brings down
the gods in such a part, but I thought Anson was too
good an artist to fall into such an error.
The Wild Cat Column.
A bank has threatened to seize Lambton (N.S.
Wales) municipal council’s electric-lighting plant, because
the council cannot pay the bank on the spot. When the
banks could not meet their engagements to the public.
Premier Dibbs and a ready Parliament stepped in and
enabled them to evade their creditors. Now, the forgiven
banking debtors catch their individual debtors by the
throat and yell—“ Pay me that thou owest.” Premier
Dibbs & Co. take no notice, because Why?
# * •
From the judge’s summing up of the Mercantile
Bank case.—“ The Crown Prosecutor had asked Davies
certain questions which might lead to an unfair impres-
sion in the minds of the jury. He had asked him the
names of the companies with which he had been con-
nected, and having got a list, elicited the fact that several
of them had failed. (Which of ’em didn’t fail ?) It was
quite possible that thousands of persons had been brought
to beggary by the failure of these companies, and ex-
tremely probable that no man regretted it more deeply
or bitterly than Davies. But until it could be proved
elsewhere that he was morally and legally liable for it
they must dismiss it from their minds altogether.” All
this time the jury was considering a balance-sheet on
which the failure of those same companies ought surely to
have been indirectly indicated, but most certainly tcam’t.
# * *
The evidence law at Melbourne and the evidence
law at Sydney appear to be widely-different things. The
N.S.W. Supreme Court, when it found a balai ce-slieet
signed by paid directors, took for granted that those
directors knew the general contents of the books upon
which the balance-sheet was founded, just as on signing
a p n. they would know for what amount it was drawn.
* * •
The 134th clause of Melb. Metropolitan Gas
Co.’s Act reads—“ The profits to be divided in any one
year shall not exceed 10 per centum on the paid-up
capital of the co.” The paid up capital has been watered
and the divs. on this diluted outlay have averaged some-
thing like twice 10 per cent, for many years. When, why,
and how was the original clause adapted to the great gas-
grab, or does it still hold good, and if so ?
* • •
Many Melbournites are economising by dis-
carding gas for kerosene—yet gas is locally but 4s. 2d.
per 1000. The electrical lighting of Melbourne by the
Corporation is a nasty knock for the Gas co., and a
prima-facie economy for the ratepayers. Let Sydney do
likewise.
• # *
The chairman of a Sydney Life Co. has dis-
covered a new currency. He severely condemns “ banks
and such ” which “inveigle people to lend them their
money, and have neither capital nor character to meet
their obligations.” But the latter is too easily counter-
feited.
* * •
“Tiger” Inglis’ attempted London flotation of
the Cremorne (Sydney Harbour) coal measures—capital
asked, £soo,ooo—seems to make headway. It was a
bright idea to hitch on to the scheme the electric-lighting
of Sydney per power developed by smail-coal.
• • •
N.S.W. Wesleyan Church is now a colliery
proprietor, having acquired by bequest 625 paid-up shares
in Mount Kembla Colliery Co. The Church does not pro-
pose to realise on these shares in the present state of the
market, and, if a cut-throat strike comes along soon, will
have a delicate part to play. Coalminers are about two-
thirds Wesleyans, where they profess any religion.
* # #
The Q. Deposit Bank has just undergone its
second reconstruction, and will doubtless go on recon-
structing till the day of judgment, since public confidence
is utterly gone, and there is no hope of doing further
business. The whole concern should have been put in
the hands of trustees, to liquidate as the property market
permitted. Then the depositors, having a preferential
claim, might by degrees get their money back; as it is,
they stand an outside show of losing the lot.
* • •
The long-promised restoration of confidence seems
still to be far away. Colonial Bank deposits are selling in
a feeble market at 13s. 3d. ; City Bank of Melbourne fetch
155.; Commercial of Australia (fixed for 10 years) are
being offered at 145.: English, Scottish, and Aus-
tralian, at 14s. 6d. ; Queensland National, at 15s. 6d. ; and
Bank of Victoria, at a shade higher. The great majority
of these “securities” carry interest at 4J per cent., so
that but for the want of confidence they ought to be in
greater demand than Government 4 per cent, stocks,
which are mostly a shade above par. It isn’t the want
of money for investment that keeps them low and almost
unsaleable, for there is so much of it that the Victorian
Government has raised the price of its debentures to
choke off the inrush of capital ; it is the want of confi-
dence, and nothing else. In face of these figures, all
the cheerful assertions that public faith has been restored
are only the crackling of a very small lot of thorns under-
neath a very large pot.
• • •
James Munro’s Real Estate Bank held another
statutory wake over its own decomposed Remains on
Thursday of last week. The report was not of the usual
despairing order, in fact it was rather hilarious than
otherwise. The shareholders have been duly crushed and
retorted, and the bank has paid a div. of 2s. in the £ out
of the results. There is now £6820 in hand against liabili-
ties of £584,000, but when the B list of contributaries is
squeezed to the uttermost, grave hopes are entertained of
another shilling, or, possibly, a shilling and a few
coppers. The prospect of 3s. in the £ for depositors is
calculated to make any Melbourne bank hilarious in these
times, and when this result is achieved, poor old James
Munro, who is now' trying to sell little suburban allot-
ments in his wooden office at Malvern, will be almost re-
habilitated in public estimation as an acute and lucky
financier.
• • •
The English and Australian Mortgage Bank
(Melbourne) announces, after a long course of liquidation,
that its liabilities are £841,780, and that, after squeezing
the shareholders dry and selling everything which
is saleable, there isn’t enough money available to
pay any div. whatever. Therefore, Judge Madden granted
the necessary authority to dig the entrails out of the
B list of contributories, who are feebly expected to
yield £5OOO. This, with sundry small sums in hand, and
anything that may be obtained by selling the big ledgers
for fertiliser, may make up a first and final dividend of 2d.
in the £, and again it may not.
• •
The Union Bank publishes a balance-sheet
which, compared to the ordinary bank-balance in these
times, is like the shadow of a great rock in an arid land.
The profits for the year (£60,295) are smaller than usual
but they are sufficient for a div. of eight per cent. The
bank has nearly £4,000,000 in available cash, and its re-
serve-fund of £1,000,000 is more discoverable than the
average reserve, the greater part of it being invested in
Govt, stocks. The deposits are nearly £17,000,000, and
the note circulation £500,000. Tbe Union Bank has now
been in existence for 57 years, and has got through every
financial storm in that long period without even a sus-
picion being cast upon its stability.
* • *
The London Bank of Australia notifies that the
first batch of reconstructed deposits, due Ist July, 1898,
will be paid at once on application.
* • •
Mr. A. J. Riley, the Sydney softgoods magnate,
ex-mayor, ex-member of both Houses of Parliament, and
once a'prospective K.C.M.G., is bankrupt. According to
his statement, he was worth £56,000 in January, ’93. In
September, ’93, he called a meeting of his creditors, being
then worth —on paper—£36,ooo. Now he shows a
shortage of over £58,000, representing a fall of £114,000
in less than 18 months. This result is attributed to the
banking panic.
Farrar & Co., the well-known Melbourne mer-
chants, have gone bankrupt; liabilities (which are always
a certainty), £98,000 ; assets (which are an awful uncer-
tainty), £lB,OOO. The unhappy arch - reconstructed
it is stated, is a creditor for £78,788, and its pros-
pective loss appears to be something like £70,000.
At last half-year’s rates this is nine months’ profits gone
in one fell swoop.
* • •
Mention of the departure of “ Lucky ” Hunt to
Coolgardie, as representative of a Sydney syndicate,
awakens the enquiry—what became of the other men who
made their piles on the Thames (M.L.) a quarter of a
century ago? De Hirsoh, now an Austrian or Russian
baton—he then kept a store at Tookey’s Hat; Thomas
Russell, C.M.G., who is said to have doubled his already
large mining fortune by a speculation in Turkish bonds at
the time of the San Stefano affair ; and “Jim ” Farmer,
who sold his thousand Caledonians at £212 a share, are
about the only really wealthy ones left. What has become
of Capt. Richards, “Pyrites” Masters and his stalwart
sons, “Manallan” Jones, long-drive Walker, lion-hearted
Tom Radford, “ Old Man ” Gribble, the Goldsworthys, the
Kneebones, Tony Obuglien, T. B. Hicks, Dick gentian,
Johnny McCabe and his mates in the Queen of Beauty ?
Grand men, every one of them. Has their lives’ quest
ended in Mullock ?

A FOSSICKER'S YARN.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
No, it’s not the price of water nor the scarcity of grub
That the fossicker discusses of an evening at the pub.,
Or underneath “ the Salmons ” where on sultry nights we
sit,
When the ivories are clicking and the billiard-lamps are
lit.
We never think of growling, we’re contented with our lot
Though with water “ bob ” a gallon, mind, it’s coming
pretty hot.
But it riles us when we read the weep that comes by every
mail
Of some jackaroo’s adventures and the agonising tale
That he’s written for his mother, and, being in the clique,
She’s had his yarn inserted in the Evening Penny Shriek.
How he travelled to “Siberia” and had to shoot his nag,
And a half-a-column drivel on the empty water-bag ;
How he slowly staggered onwards and circled in his
tracks,
And grasped the cheap revolver scenting danger from the
blacks.
And he shovels in the horror of an arid, burning plain,
With the salt-bush, dry and withered, craving hungrily
for rain;
Where he found some dying diggers, though they hadn’t
strength to ask,
Yet he gave them all the swankey that he carried in his
flask.
Then he walked the usual sixty miles without another
sup,
And led an expedition out to pick the others up.
Then he finishes his letter with the never-failing moan
How his wand'rings in the wilderness have made him skin
and bone ;
Then his mother sends a Bible, and his father sends a
cheque,
Bringing joy and consolation to the worn and battered
wreck—
Well, the truth about the matter is, these mothers’ darling
pets
Would swear they did a perish if deprived of cigarettes.
And you’ll never see these dandies leave a comfortable
job,
For they’re all of ’em connected in a manner to a nob,
Who’s got them easy billets not requiring any skill,
Though they often bang the miner when they’re beating
at the drill,
And the speculating visitor who drives a four-in-hand,
And who’s got a son or nephew in the cuS-and-collar
band,
Hysterically babbles that he’s glad to see a line
Is drawn between the gentleman and workman at the
mine,
For the reconstructed nobleman with fashionable yawn
Mustn’t tolerate advances from the lab’rer lowly born ;
So it’s when we read the papers that we get a kinder
narked
To think that by the “ Jackaroo ” the miner’s billet’s
sharked.
And when they’re playing billiards in their flannel tennis
suits
We feel like heaving something at these “ silver-tail ”
galoots ;
But the thing that makes us giggle and our temper often
cool
I* to see’em get the “black ’un ” when they’re playing
devil’s pool.
Entertainments may be scanty and our pleasures may be
few,
But we have a mild excitement in the mining “ Jackeroo,”
So we gather and discuss him, and for hours we often sit
When the ivories are clicking and the billiard lamps are lit.
Coolgardie.

Personal Items.
“I won’t undertake anything until I am
done with this case.”—Speight.
A Sydney publican is married to his fourth
wife, and has three mothers-in-law living.
“ A single stroke on the great bell of Time
may reverberate till it mingles with the last
trump.”
Rosebery was the only Englishman of dis-
tinction who patronised Berry, on his Embassy.
Took him to Mentmore.
Kidd, 1.0.G.T., is the bright exemplar of
the N.S.W. Ministry. “ Look at Kidd,” said the
admonishing Dibbs to the repentant Copeland,
“ He never acts the goat!”
Parson Edgar, of Lonsdale-st. Wesleyan
Church, Melb., boasts that he has not a sheep in
his fold who could produce more than 10s. after
paying his weekly food-bills.
Lieut.-Gov. Way (S.A.) anticipates an
earldom at the termination of his reign.
“Sammy” has twice contemptuously refused
knighthood, and will do so again.
When Mayor Manning, at Sydney official
lunch, reminded his audience that Australia had
to depend on the “old country ” for many things,
there was a loud whisper—“ Titles ! ”
Tay Pay O’Connor describes Lord Onslow
as “ a man whose talents deserve the charge of a
village post-office for their adequate area.” What
will his lordship’s late Maoriland subjects say ?
Parkes has, it is stated on good authority,
valuable interests in three lately-discovered
Westralian goldmines, the interest in one case at
least being a gift from a Westralian political ad-
mirer.
The Adelaide Supreme Court Judges are
not a happy family. Both the puisne justices are
jealous of the Chief Justice, and they deeply
resent his recent appointment as Lieut.-Governor;
for while he gets honour and emolument they
have to do his work and their own without
additional pay.
The very latest
allegation about Mrs.
Yates, the lady Mayor
of Onehunga (M.L.), is
that she doesn’t think
a sanitary inspector is
wanted, and “ drains are
not required if people
will only be a little
careful.”
Old Jeremiah Brown,
the bogus Waterloo
veteran, died last week
in Geelong (Vic.) gaol.
If he had been a genuine
Waterloo veteran, he
would probably have
died in the same place.
The gaol is always
yawning for military
veterans.

(N.S.W.) has provided
by inference a new defi-
nition of an honourable
man. In sentencing one
or two prisoners con-
victed of arson, thus
his Honor : “ It was said
that the prisoner was an
honourable man, but it
was shown that he was
constantly in arrears of
rent with his landlord.”

Mrs. RUDOLPH TE KLOOT.
Mr. RUDOLPH TE KLOOT.
It is well-known in Melb. that a rising
young barrister, who was offered a brief for the
Crown in the late Mercantile Bank case, refused
it under Walsh, but would have accepted it under
Finlay son.
A candidate for one of the Sydney Parlia-
mentary vacancies sends round his electorate a
phrenological chart of his head. The lady phre-
nologist makes it appear that this candidate is the
one man out of a generation.
George Grey has left for England, and
doesn’t know when he will come back. An im-
mense crowd bade him farewell at Auckland when
the Gothic left on March 8. Queen Victoria has
long expressed a desire to talk with the ancient.
Miss Bonynge, to whom our old friend*
Lord Deerhurst, has been married in London with
great splendour, is the adopted daughter of mil-
lionaire Bonynge just as Miss Maokay (now Prin-
cess Colonna) is the adopted daughter of Bonanza
Mackay.
“ I’ll make a peer of him,” she said,
“ Who peers has made of many.”
Old Gladstone shook his heavy head ;
“ No, thank you, ma’am —not any.
Fools I’ve made peers, to be my tools,
Now, you would make me peer of fools !”
An Australian Judge, conversing with a
protninent barrister the other day, said, “You
are likely to be made acting-judge in a couple of
days. Take my cue. When you go on the bench
be a terror. Punish to the limit of your power.
Afterwards you can be just. But begin by being
Draco.”
001. Sec. Tozer, the bugbear of Queens-
land unionists, and the , hardest-working member
of the Ministry, comes south for a holiday in
Maoriland. He tells you confidentially that his
health was broken in the shearers’ war, when,
“ single-handed, sir, I fought and quelled the
army of anarchy.”
Jabez Balfour, now in prison at Buenos
Ayres awaiting extradition, says that he “has
entire confidence in'the justice of God.” Evi-
dently he expects to be hanged, but as he can’t get
more than penal servitude for life it would have
been more accurate if Jabez had only expressed
partial confidence.
The last words of, the good-natured 0. J.
Roberts, M.L.0., wringing the hands of ac-
quaintances on board the English-bound boat at
Sydney wharf, were that he was “off Home.”
And yet Mr. Roberts first saw the light in Sydney,
and could hardly have risen to be Postmaster-
General in any other civilised land.
Ambrose Hallen, the blind candidate for
Sherbrooke (N.S.W.), is in many respects a
remarkable man. Single-handed he has reclaimed
from the wilderness a farm of 12 acres, and clears
<£4oo a year at poultry-rearing. His place is the
prize poultry-farm of the province. Hallen is a
fair speaker, and has a wonderful memory.
English Daily News tells of a new North
Country Mayor who, in returning thanks, said—
“ln ray opinion, a mayor should be like Csesar’s
wife, all things to all men.” Maryborough (Q.)
News caps this with the speech of a member of
Bundaberg Ferry Board : “ The fact is, a ferry-
man should be like Potiphar’s wife, above sus-
picion.”
Alfred Deakin, brightest of native-born
statesmen, when much in evidence politically
visited a Vic. up-country show and lectured on
“ Irrigation.” Next day, two farmers met in the
street, and, talking over the show, one said:
“ I see Deakin was at the show yesterday.”
“ Deakin ! ” said the other, “ whose horse is he? ”
Such is fame.
The irrepressible Mortimer Franklyn is
not dead; au contraire, he is very much to the
fore as agent for the Mutual Life Insurance Co. of
New York. He is making over .£4OOO a year. A
rival insurance man told The Bulletin’s London
correspondent that Mortimer some time since took
Liverpool by storm and insured every man who
was worth insuring in the place. He is considered
one of the smartest men in the business.

Journal by Caldwell, S. Australian M.P., about
the local Methodist Conference, fails to extinguish
the author at next election, there is no justice left
in the land. The first verse :
The Wesleyan Methodist Conference
Has Just began to meet
In the central sanctuary Church,
Which stands in Pirie-street.
It is quite Qjl the cards that Griffith will
resign his judgeship arid return to the political
arena. Judge Harding cannot forgive his being
passed over, and seldom misses a chance to be dis-
agreeable, as witness his dig at S.W.G. re the
Court being' “made a' puppet of in insolvency
cases.” Fancy Harding being made a puppet!
Why, he once gave a man “40 years” fur con-
tempt, for coming into Court with a revolver in
his pocket.
Colonel Miller, the father of Money and
Mars of that ilk, was a man so jealous as to be
almost monomaniacal. In the fifties he made a
second marriage with one of the acknowledged
belles of Hobart, a good and charming lady,
but always walked about three yards behind
her in the street to keep a watch on the
“ youth who winked a roving eye.” The pro-
cession was one of the principal amusements of
the sleepful village.
Passage from Romilly’s reminiscences
the West Pacific dealing with New Britain :
The mate then said i “ I don’t so much mind leaving the
captain behind, as he has a boat, but, unfortunately, he
has left his only pair of trousers on board, and I'm afraid
he won’t like to go on board a man-of-war to see you
gentlemen without them.” “ But,” said we, “ has he not
gone to call upon a lady?” “ Oh,” said the mate, “ she
won’t mind ; her husband never wears trousers, and
there is nothing she hates so much as a man that gives
himself airs.”
Phil May, The Bulletin hears, has an
atelier in Paris, where he now is, in addition to
his studio in Kensington. He intends to devote
himself to “serious” work over there, but will
alternate lighter business, to be done in London.
“ His artist friends think he is wasting his un-
doubted genius on black and white, which, how-
ever well it pays, will not bring him fame” —
which is mere bosh. By the way, he is now on
the regular staff of Punch, and has given up
working for Sketch, though they have a lot of
stuff of his which will appear from time to time.
Judge Seth Smith, of Maoriland, has
resigned and is going back to England, and the
country at large is going down to the wharf to
see him off. Judge Smith is a wonderful example
of the advantages of having an uncle. He
was the nephew of an imported British
bishop, and followed his uncle the bishop to
Maoriland. There he was promptly made a
magistrate. Like almost all men named Seth he
was a gloomy and morose Christian, with a
boiler - plated creed of the kind that leads
to much damnation of unbaptised infants.
By-and-by be rose, through his influence with
the boiler-plated Christian community, to be
a Judge of the Native Land Court, and as
he knew little of the Maori language, or of
Maori land tenure, or of Maori habits, customs,
or modes of thought, he was a great and wonder-
ful failure. Most of his confused and inextricable
judgments deserve to be preserved in spirits to
show what a bishop’s nephew is capable of.
As an example of the utter hopelessness of being
named Seth, however, he was eminently successful
as all times. Seth, as a front name, is only one
degree less hopeless than Jabez.
“Collectors of Australian books,” writes
The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ will
shortly have a rare chance of securing some gems,
as Henniker Heaton has made up his mind to
bring his valuable library to the hammer. Re
began collecting many years ago, when he was a
poor man, and, fortunately for him, before the
fashion for Australian literature set in. He
was consequently enabled to pick up all kinds
of rare editions, and numbers of duplicates. His
collection contains many splendid works, some of
them unique, notably De Quiros, of which he
possesses the only copy known to exist, and for
which there will be keen competition. The book
is worth hundreds. Having so many irons in the
fire, he finds that he cannot spare time to go book-
hunting as he used to do, and, being unable to
keep his collection up to date, he has determined
to dispose of it.”
The Bulletin prints portraits of the
parties in the notorious Te Kloot divorce-ease,
heard at Sydney, last week—Rudolph Oscar
Randwick Te Kloot, petitioner ; Florence Mary
Isabel Te Kloot, respondent; and John Edye
Manning, co-respondent. The jury gave a
majority verdict for petitioner on the ground of
adultery, with .£2OOO damages against co-respon-
dent, to be settled on respondent and her child !
The evidence was so unsavoury that the Chief
Justice prohibited its publication ; and a feature
in the case was the unmeasured manner in which
his Honor denounced the brutality of the private
detectives employed. Manning, the co-respon-
dent, who is very highly connected, is 60 years of
age, and the father of a family. He “protected ”
the young couple, and in 1892 found employment
for petitioner in the Civil Service; before, as
the Judge phrased it, “acting the part of a
scoundrel.”
W. S. Dowel’s address to the electors of Bin-
gara (N.S.W.) seemingly cribs a verse of Brunton
Stephens’ poem “The Dominion of Australia,”
which is mutilated and printed as a prose perora-
tion without acknowledgement. Otherwise, the
address is a copy of that which bamboozled Tam-
worth at last election. Thus Dowel :
The dawning light tinges the hills with the grandeur of
our destiny, and the great national life that must be
embodied in the future commonwealth that creates Aus-
tralia a nation, great, glorious, and free. Not yet her
day. How long “not vet?” There comes the flush of
violet, and heavenward faces all aflame with sanguine
imminence of morn wait but the sun rise to proclaim the
day of our great nation born.
Thus Stephens :
Not yet her day. How long “not yet?”
There comes the flush of violet
And heavenward faces all aflame
With sanguine imminence of morn
Wait but the sun-kiss to proclaim
The Day of the Dominion born.
Prelusive baptism, &o.
Parkes hereafter may be famous as the
only man who launched out with a couple of
advts. in Wells’ Gazetteer of the Australian
Colonies, published in Sydney, 1848. Turning up
the very few advts. in this ancient volume one
finds : —“Henry Parkes—lvory and Bone Turner,
&c. Manufacturer of Billiard and Bagatelle balls,
Chess and Backgammon men, Dice, Crochet
needles, Dog whistles, Ivory thimbles, &0., &o.
Dealer in English and Foreign Toys and fancy
articles. Henry Parkes, No. 20 Hunter St., near
George St., Sydney.” Upon the page facing
’Enry’s principal manifesto is a smaller advt. in-
viting inspection of his Old English Dolls, Whales’
teeth, &c., &c. As a dealer in nursery goods he
acquired, perhaps, the germs of inspiration for his
baby poems of 40 years later —“ Little doggie on a
little loggie,” and so forth. Yet the old man’s
history of Parkes and N.S.W. makes no reference
to his early connection with dolls and dog whistles.
Before setting up in his humble Hunter-st. shop,
Henry had done indifferently well as a “tide-
waiter.” It was not till he entered Parliament
that he thoroughly mastered the art of catching
tides on the turn.
Queensland Full Court’s decision that
driving a cart outside a plantation is not legally
“field work ” cuts at the root of one of the worst
black-labour abuses—the employment of kanakas
in unlawful avocations to the detriment of white
labourers.
An average of 20 to 30 men “ who have
seen better days ” call at Melbourne morgue every
morning to know if “ there’s anything on to-day.”
The fact that only 4s. “ a case ” is allowed a jury-
man again emphasises the marvellous enterprise
of the unemployed Victorian.
Angus and Robertson, Sydney, send two
illustrated Tory pamphlets dealing with Gladstone
and the Irish question. W.E.G., with Parnell,
Dillon, Davitt, and other Irish loaders, have all
their faults observed, set in a note-book, learned
and conned by rote. drawings and letterpessr
are ingenious and clever, and thoroughly partisan.
Dr Parker, the greatNonoonformistparson,
according to an interview in the Idler, “is con-
tinually giving sums of money away, of which
none but he and the recipient know anything
whatever.” Good old advertising Samaritan !
The story is horribly suggestive of that legendary
Irish advt.: “Whereas Patrick Maloney has
stolen my pig without my knowledge, if he doesn’t
return it his name will be published.”
Prodigious efforts were made to induce
Forde and Green, the two phantom Vic. Railway
Commissioners “ suspended ” along with Speight,
to bring actions against the Age on their little
accounts. But one of them, at least, had a good
deal of money to lose by going to law, and they
decided not to move. Allison Smith, the engine-
man, has chipped into the game, however. His
libel claim for <£15,000 has been served upon David
Syme, and people are beginning to feel sorry for
the beleaguered newspaper proprietor. Whoever
the Age may, or may not, have libelled, it is quite
certain that the lawyers, to whom Syme has
never done any damage, are making him suffer
unduly. Before these railway cases are done with
the pig-headed public may begin to understand
that the civil law is an accursed jaggedly weapon
made for themselves by the few who are licensed
to use it. Truly the Anarchist isn’t so very mad,
after all. He objects to Law,
Ata recent Northern
Circuit sittings, a perspir-
ing young counsel, scion
of a big legal family, had
just begun to address the
Court on behalf of pri-
soner, when he was
tapped on the shoulder.
Turning round to receive,
as he imagined, fresh
instructions from his
client, he was met with :
“ For God’s sake, young
man, sit down ; I could
do better myself !”

brutal circumstances The lives of peaceabl ■,

POINTS.
A cloud on the N.S.W. horizon—a Dibbs-
Bruce Smith coalition.
How much British secret service money
has been spent in Australia?
“ The born peer has 20 years’ start in
English politics.” How long, O Lord, how long ?
Australia would like penny postage to
England, or anywhere else, but is not prepared to
pay for it just now.
There is phylloxera in the Lord’s vineyard.
All the hard-up parsons are trying to rush seats in
N.S.W. Assembly.
“Dick” Haynes (brother to Mudgee
1 Haynes) is opposing the re-election of Minister
Quinlan for West Perth.
The commissioners had, by making the
N.S.W. railways payable, practically reduced the
national debt by £3s,ooo,ooo.—Bruce Smith.
M‘II wraith’s recent interviewing in
London re the Land-grant railways is merely the
pilot-fish that heralds the approach of the shark.
Two hundred homeless and half-starved
men were turned out of Adelaide Exhibition
grounds to make room for the fat, over-fed pigs
down for the show.
What is the meaning of the fact that the
“fashionable” element of the N.S.W. Female
Suffragists openly “ barracks ” for the so-called
“Freetrade ” cause ?
“In all democracies the true and wise
democrat, in the best interests of his cause, seeks
to establish and to render strong a Chamber of
Second Thoughts.”— Argus.
“The proposal to take food from the
mouths of honest, struggling citizens, so as to
put work into the hands of State-fed rogues and
vagabonds, is outrageous.”— Age.
Significant 1 There is only one titled
Premier in Australasia, but the leaders of the
Opposition—Berry, Braddon, Downer, and Parkes
—in four provinces are be-baubled.
A political prophecy to which the lat e
Parnell committed himself unreservedly in his last
days was that Haroourt was to be Gladstone’s
successor. He did not take Rosebery seriously.

to go to gaol in a body was met by a N.S.W.
M.L.0., with the last argument of kings. “We’ll
flog them,” he said. “ Bring them on ; we’ll flog
them.”

The Sydney police take much trouble to
catch the illicit distiller. But they take no trouble
to suppress the stuff he puts on the market.
Good grog—how can it be profitably sold at 3d. a
drink ?

and one of the first to suggest itself, is to engage
them upon\ public works : on works Which, if not
undertaken by convicts, would not be undertaken
at all.”—Age.

(Kanaka champions) form part of a deputation to

Ah Sin, a Charters Towers gardener who
makes a nice thing out of oranges, has a white
man and a blackfellow working side by side in his
garden. They get each Is. a day and “tucker.”
One of the things that Miss Floral Shaw didn’t
see in the tropics.
Rumoured that Premier Dibbs intends to
legally injunct John Davies, M.L.C., from putting
up a bust to him in the new Sydney Hospital.
Considering how very rough it is upon any man
to have his bust erected by John Davies, there is
some probability about the rumour.
Writes a Northern planter :—“ Out of 50
Kanakas landed on the Herbert not long ago, 14
were unfit for work and four were dying. The
others were eventually sent home. A vessel
recently brought seven under age, and an attempt
was made to get rid of them at reduced fees!"
Dr. Renwick, writes The Bulletin’s Lon-
don correspondent by last mail, sails for Sydney
by next Orient steamer. He intends to resign bis
seat in N.S.W. Upper House, and will contest a
popular seat. He has had several requisitions,
but, in all probability, will stand for a metropoli-
tan constituency.
The Argus on the vanity of relief works :
“ There can be no greater misfortune. When re-
lief works were initiated at Sydney, a few years
back, the whole loaferdom of Australia collected
together at Port Jackson.” Then the man who
runs from one end of Australia to another after
a miserable job is a loafer?
Symon, Q.C., is anxious to re-enter S.A.
Parliament. His present goal is the Council.
Should he enter the Assembly, there would be
bitter duels between him and Premier Kingston,
who have cordially hated each other ever since
they were articled clerks together in the office of
Mr. (now Chief Justice) Way.
It is only a question of sticking to the
Colonial Office and giving Lord Ripon no peace,
remarks The Bulletin’s London correspondent,
for the captain of the Costa Rica Packet to get
,it compensation out of the Dutch Government;
r L« the claim for £25.000 is too stiff, and, if per-
sisted in, will inevitably -lead to a refusal to enter-
tain it.
One phase of the Polynesian Labourers’
Act seldom noticed is that it is practically an Act
for the impoverishment of the soil. To reap the
benefit of the above Act a man must grow sugar—
and sugar exclusively. Result—the Mary River
is lined with abandoned plantations—ground
worked-out, cane sick. And other districts are
following suit.
Working-men are frequently upbraided
for being led away by “ paid agitators.” At all
events, their agitators are paid. Fat Man cheats
his agitator whenever he can; as witness the
Patriotic League (National Ass) in Brisbane,
where the sec.’s salary is months in arrear, while
the wealthy members are conveniently deaf to the
poor wretch’s howls to pay up subscriptions.
An instructive deputation has just worried
the Yictowian Ministaw of Wail ways about the
civil servants, so one naturally drifts into the
Grocaw, Bakaw, Butchaw, and Tailaw language.
The first six weeks of this year—or yaw—yielded
19 insolvencies in the Railway Department alone,
and the deputation was of indignant, blighted,
swindled tradesmen, whining that this was too
much.
Queensland Worker :—“lt is possible to
work up an agitation in the North and Centre
which might shake the MTlwraith party . . .
but such an agitation is not one that the workers
in the South would grow very enthusiastic over.”
If the workers in the South will not grow enthusi-
astic over an agitation which will upset their
direst enemy, their condition is, indeed, past
praying for.
London Times declares that George Dibbs
has been discourteous to the Imperial, treasury.
Yes, and the same bad boy has been discourteous
to Chicago. In course of trine he will have given
every continent and country a turn. Then there
will be an anti-Dibbs Federation League started,
which will bind together the whole civilised and
uncivilised peoples of the world. Finally, Dibbs
will be dethroned and Crick made governor of the
visible universe.

Victorian Premier Patterson : “ Many of
the men who are now, for mere party purposes,
directing agitations against the Government will,
if they are not a little more careful, create some-
thing which will alarm them when it occurs.”
Patterson delights in this sort of cheap and com-
bustible awfulness, but thecountry tires of waiting
for the dreadful occurrence which is always going
to eventuate, but which never takes place.
The Freetrade candidate for a Sydney
suburb begs voters to forgive him for his Protec-
tionist lapse of five years ago, on the ground that
“what I did once”—one little time and then no
more—“Parkes did for two years continuously
and has been fully pardoned for.” Is this to begin
a new apologetic era in which Parkes’ protracted
lapses of various kinds are to stand as excuses for
others to enjoy once—one little time —a much-
loved lapse with impunity?
Mrs. Leontine Cooper, of Brisbane (Q.),
has shaken her skirts at and abandoned the Q.
Woman’s Rights Society, because it asks for
“One-woman-one-vote.” Madame is a “littery
lydy,” and she is above eleetorally consorting with
any female “below her degree.” As a “lydy
patroness ” of the common female, she knows her
“spear,” but as a comrade of that female —
“ shudder me into hysterics were I not so master-
fully unfeminine ! ” says echo.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley, with
Justices Windeyer, Innes, Stephen and Foster,
sat lately on The Queen v. O’Keefe case. O’Keefe
bad been tried and sentenced at Quarter Sessions,
but on a technical point was released, and the
question now raised was whether he could be
re-tried on the original charge. Now, seeing that
barrister Moriarty is sole spokesman on O’Keefe’s
side, why is the Crown represented by F. E.
Rogers, Q. 0., Barton, Q. 0., and barrister
Dawson ? Such a bar must have cost £lOO.
Either there is a studied effort to produce a cer-
tain legal result or there is a manifest waste of
public money.
The Postal Conference in Maoriland ex-
pressed an opinion unfavourable to the employ-
ment of coloured crews on the P. and O. mail
steamers, but took no official action. Is there no
democrat with spunk enough to move a stoppage
of the Australian subsidy unless the evil is
mended? If the Australias refused to enter into
any contract unless white labour was employed,
the steamship co. would soon come to terms.
Meanwhile, every believer in a white Australia
should support the Orient Co., which, in many
respects, gives the better service, employs all
white crews, and has broken down an ancient
monopoly.
“Prisoner, you are a lucky man. You
are discharged. It is the jury’s verdict and not
mine that sets you free. I believe every word
sworn against you. Go.” This set speech with
but a few unimportant verbal variations is de-
livered in N.S.W. Supreme and District Courts
on an average about once a month. What does
the practice indicate? Well, that soon it will be
a burning question whether the jury system will
be abolished, or Judges be restrained. Which
shall it be ? There can only be one answer.
W.A. democratic election
programme, as submitted by D«
Hamel, at Yilgarn, includes
repeal of the stock-tax (30 per
cent, per head on imported bul-
locks, by which the Govt,
graziers keep up the price of
meat); other necessaries of life
to be duty-free; water conser-
vation on goldfields, cheap rail-
ways, electoral reform (compris-
ing one-man-one-vote, one-day
elections, triennial Parliaments,
payment of members, simplifi-
cation of registry and transfer),
land reform (including classifi-
cation of land-values with prices
accordingly, selection before
survey and assistance to settlers,
tax on unimproved land, burst-
up of big estates) and economy
generally.
Ministerial replies to mem-
ber Kelly, in N.S.W. Assembly,
disclose that under general
orders applicants for commis-
sions in the local Permanent
Artillery must pass a Univer-
sity examination. Six names
were handed in ; one candidate
passed. A son of Colonel Spald-
ing, who did not pass, was
thereupon attached to the Per-
manent Artillery at .£l2 per
month and allowances; while a
son of Major Airey, who also
failed to pass, was made Acting-
Adjutant of the 2nd Infantry
regiment. Needless to say that
the other unsuccessful candi-
dates were not appointed to
anything. The military caste is
not merely a caste ; it is an
hereditary caste; and the na-
tion which has billeted the
fathers is to be billeted with
the sons also.
In Maoriland, as in N.S.
Wales, imprisonment for debt
has been abolished. When a
man owes money, and the court
orders him to pay it, and he is
not able to pay, he isn’t impri-
soned for the debt but for con-
tempt of court. Year before
last, 749 persons were not im-
prisoned for debt in Maoriland,
butonly for disobeying the court.
Maoriland is a progressive
country, and the law would
shudder at the obtuse barbarity
of shutting a manup in gaol be-
cauaehe is unable to pay. It
only shuts him up because he doesn’t pay.

NATIONAL EXPENDITURE.
(Relic of nineteenth century civilisation found
carved on an ancient monument, A.D. 3500.)
Twenty millions for the churches
Where the ’stablished parson perches;
Forty millions for the gory
Ones who wade to death or glo’y—
Several millions for the pauper and a hundred for
the war;
That the peers may live in plenty
Pensions up to millions twenty ;
And, to oil the nation’s throttle,
Thirty millions for the bottle;
Some few millions for His Majesty, and ten score
millions more
For the classes
Who employ the toiling masses ;
Hundred millions then for rental,
And (if then you haven’t spent all)
Just a trifle then to hury those who die beside your
door.
Q.
P. LUFTIG.
The Sydney Wool Market.
Thb wool season being now at an end only odd lots and
small parcels are received lor sale by the various wool-
brokers. For the convenience of buyers sales are held
fortnightly instead of weekly as in the rush of the season.
This arrangement enables buyers from the Southern
capital to attend our sales, and, consequently, compeli
tion must be increased by their presence. Recent news
from London quotes the market at 5 per cent, lower, and
at the sales this week the market showed a decided
decline in sympathy.
M'Burney, Wallis and Co. held over their catalogue
because they thought it unwise to entrust owners’ in-
terests on the market in the face of such discouraging
news from their London agents.
The results of other brokers’ sales go to show’ that this
firm was correct in their decision not to offer. By hold-
ing for another fortnight, they give the market ample
time to recover. The new firm are to he commended for
their careful protection of small lines, it being a great mis-
take for brokers to rush wool upon the market simply
because the sale-day has come round. Small lots should
receive the same consideration os large, and M'Burney,
Wallis and Co.’s good judgment this week proves that
they have the welfare of their constituents at heart.;

THE LEGISLATIVE COUNCIL.
In the Legislative Council last evening,
before the dinner-hour, l»r. MacLaunu
arranged that tho Bathing Regulation Bill
should be taken before the further conside-
ration of the Mining on Private Lands Bill,
the only other item of Government business
on the notice paper
One of the provisions of the bill was that all
bathers should be clothed “ from the neck to
the knee,’ Mr.
Hangar, in committee, moved the omission
of the words ** from the neck to the knee
from the bill, and called for a division. It
was found that the uyes and the noes were
even, and the chairman gave his casting vote
in favor of the retention of the words. The
bill was reported to the House without
amendment. Dr. MacLaurin then thought it
7 was 100 late to do anything with the Mining
'lon Private Lands Bill, and the House ad-
journed, shortly before 9 o’clock,till Wed-
nesday next.

Society.
When a “swell” down South lately got
six months for fraud, the members of his family
marked the event by going into mourning. Here
is a new idea which is far more logical, in a pro-
fessedly Christian community, than mourning for
the dead.
# # •
The Female Vanguard of Reform already
quarrel as to whether the title of a lady, who may
be elected chief local magistrate, shall be, “Her
Worship the Mayoress,” or “Her Worship the
Mayor.” And what is to be the official title of
the husband of “ Her Worshipful?” When a man
becomes Mayor, -his wife becomes Mayoress. But
when a woman becomes Mayor, what does her
husband become? Answer—a mere cypher.
• * •
Whence do Judges derive the power to
forbid the publication of evidence, as Chief Justice
Darley forbade it in the divorce-case of Te Kloob
versus Te Kloot, heard in Sydney, last week?
First, the Chief Justice ordered that the evidence
should not be published till the case closed, and
afterwards he directed that it should not be
published at all. The point is not whether or not
the order benefited public decency; it is, whether
or not a judge, qua judge, sits invested with the
3»ower thus exercised. In England, George
Jessell, Master of the Rolls, and other eminent
jurists, declare that a judge possesses no such legal
authority, and argue that if he may suppress
evidence because it appears this, that, or the
other, he can suppress all evidence at all times.
* * *
Doesn’t Windeyer, J., give his profession
badly away by assuming that it doesn’t normally
take the view expressed by the late Judge Higin-
botliam when he said that the primary duty of a
defending counsel is, not to secure his client’s
acquittal, right or wrong, but to take care that
accused shall not suffer from inadequate presenta-
tion of the facts in his favour ?
* # *
“ Meet me in church. I’ll keep right
hand on top ledge of pew.” Thus ran an advt. in
a recent Sydney paper ; but after one edition
of the journal was published, the formes were
overhauled and other matter substituted.
Everyone knows
or should, thesehard
times—the aggres-
sive-looking, knotty
walking-stick that a
bailiff always carries.
It is as much part and
pared of him as his
shiny, greasy coat and
stale - beer - laden
breath, and when he
taps gently at your
front door, keeping
well out of eye-range
of the upstairs-win-
dows, he leans non-
chalantly on that
stick and patiently
waits. Open the door
ever so little and the
mischief is done ; for,
as he bids you the
time o’ day, he sur-
reptitiously inserts
the point of that stick
between thedoor edge
—near the hinges—
and the door -jamb,
and nothing short of
breaking the door will
close it. Then he
whistles to his mate,
hiding round the
corner, plants his hoof
in the passage, with-
draws the stick,
breathes hard on you
and you collapse.
Most people are under
the impression that
the “bummer” car-
ries a walking-stick
for protection. They
are wrong. His
breath is his protec-
tion ; the stick is
merely his implement
of “ trade.”

The organists of
•the five R.C. churches
in Brisbane are
women, to a man;
and yet, so far, there
has been no “shock-
ing scandal.”
The Sydney police revolver idiocy keeps
burstinv out into fresh problems every day. In
the ease of the man whom the Woolloomooloo
policeman threatened to blow a hole through if he
didn’t come from under a bed to be collared : Is
the “cooinan” iustified in pistolling his suspect
when he funks “ going to ground ” after him. If
not, has any be-buttoned barbarian from Bally-
blatherskite more right than the civilised citizen
to threaten “grievous bodily harm”? If not,
again—isn’t it about time the non-hysterioal end
of the community came down squash on this stupid
concession to the nine-day shriekists ?
* * *
The Bulletin is ready to bet, retire recent
arming of the Sydney police with pistols, that
a policeman will commit suicide with his revolver,
or shoot a fellow-constable or an innocent citizen,
or be shot with his own weapon by some criminal
who has snatched it, before any bona-fide burglar
or murderer has bitten the dust per medium of the
newly served-out weapons.
* * *
Waverley (Sydney) women’s deputation—
to Railway Commissioners. “Tram-fare_ to
Bondi-Junction is 3d. ; to Woollahra terminus
(same distance), only 2d. Equalise matters ! ”
Commissioners: “Certainly! We’ll charge the
Woollahra people a penny more ! ” Which recalls
a story of Armstrong, the gun-builder. He had a
clerk, no end of a swell, who complained, in an
imperial sort of way, that his desk was too low.
Armstrong: “What remedy do you suggest? ”
“Oh, a highah desk!” Armstrong: “There’s
another I—l’ll1 —I’ll get a shorter clerk ! ”
* * *
The only objectors to the electric-light in
Melbourne are the Gas Co. and the demi-monde.
The former dislike it because it interferes with
their business, and the latter because —well, think
it out for yourself.
# • *
An old member sends this to The Bul-
letin : “The Australian Club, Meloourne, is now
run on the lines of a second-class hotel. Its sub-
«oription-fee has been reduced from £5O to £lO.
Each member is allowed to introduce a friend
daily to dinner. It is contemplated giving to
each member permission to introduce a friend to
sleep, breakfast, and lunch at the club. This
latter will obviate the further introduction of
fresh members, as strangers will practically have
all the privileges of members without paying the
entrance-fee. The committee, in view of the latter
innovation, are having the usual notice posted in
the rooms freeing themselves from responsibility
for any theft which may be committed, as in
ordinary hotels.”
* * *
Sydney Mayor’s luncheon was chiefly re-
markable for the ludicrous inconsequence of the
remarks made by travelling-philanthropist Char-
rington. No more absurd oration has ever been
heard under the roof of the building.
# * •
One of the divorce-cases down for hearing
in an Australian court turns on the husband’s
allegation that his wife persisted in getting her
hair dressed by a male hair-dresser. The husband
fortifies his case with proof that his wife objected
to his having his own hair dressed by a female
hair-dresser. *

Dear Bulletin, — Re Bulletin par., 3rd
March, about the man who, having made a good
race-meeting win, thoughtfully ordered (and paid
for) his own grave-lot and tombstone, you
are slightly in error. The tombstone in question
remained in the monumental mason’s yard till
recently, when its owner got married again, and,
with a delicacy and a presence of mind which did
him infinite credit, stowed it away for future use.
“ Look at me,”
cried a lately-elected
Sydney suburban
alderman, addressing
the cheering crowd.
“I never drank,
never gambled, and
—here I am ! This
is a reward which
pays a man in full
for a lifetime of self-
discipline and self-
restraint ! Here I
am to-day a proud,
proud man ! ”
DOUBTFUL SANITY.
ADVENTUROUS HUSBAND (100 feet above sea):
“It’s all right, dear, if I did fall I
can swim.”
ANXIOUS WIFE : “Don’t speak so loud. Such
a remark might invalidate your will.”

* * •
Nepotism seems rife in the N.S.W. Per-
manent Artillery. The Imperial-Australian pro-
fessional military circle already constitutes an
arrogant and poisonous caste, for whom there is a
rough time ahead at the hands of the Democratic-
party.
* * •
A new industry has sprung up on the
southern coast. The principal artist is an accom-
plished female, speaking several languages, and
travelling in search of health with monsieur her
husband. Solo-whist is her forte, but she is not
particular as to the game. A well-known auto-
cratic Judge and his associate dropped over a hun-
dred to the pair a few weeks back. First “ solo,”
and then baccarat. His Honor paid up smilingly
and thought it was luck. Sometimes madame
wins, sometimes monsieur, but they get there just
the same.
# * •
The accomplished Mr. Julian Ashton
places himself in a wrong and undignified position
by remaining trustee of N.S.W. National Art
Gallery while continuing in professional practice
as an artist. It is not enough for Mr. Ashton to
say that he do sn’t judge his own pictures, or to
plead (in effect) that even if he does judge his
rival artists’ pictures he judges them justly. Mr.
Justice Innes is also a member of the art-trust,
but he would decline to sit on the Supreme Court
Bench in any case in which he was even colorably
interested. Mr. Ashton, being human, must have
at least a colorable personal interest in his brother
artists’ work. He should, therefore, resign
either his trusteeship or his professional practice.
* * *
The prudent regulation which prevents
Ms.P. from becoming Government contractors
should apply abo in the case of Mr. Julian Ash-
ton’s N.S.W, Art Gallery trusteeship. Either he
should not be a trustee or he should not sell pic-
tures to the trustees. As long as the connection
continues, it will be a scandal and a stumbling-
block, however unreasonably, to both Mr. Ashton
and his colleagues.
# # *
A metropolitan temperance hotel-keeper,
with his business edge finely sharpened, wrote to
the clerics who have been holding assemblies and
conferences in Sydney lately, inviting them to put
up at his palace, and stating that payment for the
accommodation need not be made till they have
settled down again in their country homes.
Minister for Education Suttor (N.S.W.)
has just refused to sanction the taking up of a
collection among the much - retrenched female
school teacher’s for the purpose of testirnouialising
Under-Secretary Johnson, whose present lament-
able state of health is not unlikely to enforce his
retirement in the near future. By the way, the
moneys collected for another official testimonial
which didn’t come off, and which were long sup-
pi isfd to have been returned, have never, in the
case of some, of the country subscribers, been
received to this day, despite repeated applications
for same.

Why should a solici-
tor to whom a hundred
or a thousand pounds is
handed for a specific
purpose, and who mis-
applies that money, ever
be allowed “ time?” His
position as an “ officer of
the Supreme Court”
makes him more than
the trusted employe of
the man who remits, and
more than the trusted
bailee of the man who is
supposed to receive. If
a mercantile clerk mis-
applied money entrusted
to him by his employer
he would, in plain
language, be promptly
“lagged.” The Supreme
Court is an institution
which often privileges
Thieves I—with 1 —with a capital
T. The attorney who
embezzles —for that is the
word—his client’s money
should go to gaol,
whether he pays up or
whether he doesn’t.
The N.S. Wales Instruction Department
authorities now find that their regulation, decree-
ing that female teachers, upon marrying, vnu-t
leave the service, cannot be legally enforced
unless all moneys and interest thereon paid into
the Superannuation Fund by such teachers are
refunded—and the N.S.W. Superannuation Fond
doesn’t feel well enough to stand that. However,
in some instances, the mere “bluff” has had the
desired effect, and a few teachers have sent in
their resignations, which were eagerly accepted.
One lady, when applying for a Sydney school,
stated incidentally that it would be but for a few
months, as she was shortly to be married. She
mentioned the date, which the department must
have noted, for soon after the stated day she
received an official letter reminding her that her
resignation had not been received. But the mar-
riage has been postponed indefinitely, and the
department is still whistling for her resignation.
# * *
A Melb. paper points out, as a matter of
interest to K.C.M.G.’s and others, that the wife
of a common knight is not a “ Lady ” at all, but
only a “ Dame.” Therefore, when she calls herself
“ Lady ” she assumes a bogus title, and when she
airs her sham distinction at Govmentouse it is
the duty of the Private-Secretary to suppress
her. “Dame,” regarded as a title, sounds tolerably
common, and is only a shade better than that
worn by the ancient crone who used to be called
“ Old Goody Smith ” in English village-tales.
* * *
Melbourne was lighted by electricity last
week, the current being formally turned on by
the Mayoress. Then “ the company adjourned to
the Town Hall and partook of refreshments.”
They always do, somehow. It is one of the worst
features in the Anglo-Saxon’s character that on
every important or unimportant occasion he par-
takes of refreshments. He never does anything
without partaking of refreshments. Whether he
is struck by a great happiness or a great disaster
it is all one—he immediately partakes of refresh-
ments. When the Last Day comes the last of the
race will celebrate the occasion by partaking of
refreshments all by himself. It is in the blood.
Refreshments are the last thing the man of British
descent has before he is hanged. If he is
going to be flogged he has refreshments. When
the doctor sounds him and tells him that he has
got a mortal disease which will presently cut him
off, he sets his teeth hard so as to conceal any
trace of emotion, and- goes out and partakes of
refreshments. He greets every visitor of unusual
importance by trying to make him eat more than
is good for him, and he bids farewell to every
departing friend by giving him a horrible bilious
attack. There is nobody else on this earth who is
so consistent and so persevering in the matter of
refreshments. Refreshments are his god. It is
generally supposed that money is his god, but it
isn’t; he only values money as a means towards
partaking of refreshments.
* # *
The winner of a recent big prize told a
Hebe at a fashionable hotel, when he bought his
ticket, that if successful he would give her .£lOOO
of it —and he has kept his word.
• * *
The most patient woman on earth obtained
a judicial separation last week in Sydney. She
was married in ’55, and two years later her hus-
band deserted her for another woman. He has
stuck to the other woman ever since, and his wife,
after 37 years of solitary communing with herself,
reckoned that she had waited long enough, and
applied to the court for relief. The Judge—by
way of hidden humour, mayhi—ordered the
question of alimony to stand over.

" London. Monday Night.—Miss Hopkins, an American
heiress who was staying at Leamington Spa, a fashionable
watering-place 20 miles south-east of Birmingham, has
married a policeman whom she hah known only a month.
She adopted this [?] to avoid a distasteful marriage to
which she was being urged by her friends.”— CABLE.
The eloping Tasmanian pair are said to
have settled down in Queensland.
# • •
If a man were to stand up in a public
place and repeat clearly and audibly and without
the monotonous whine or canting snuffle with
which it is too often associated, the first chapter
of Isaiah, most of his hearers would imagine it
was a piece of impassioned prose written about the
Melbourne of to day. Every accusation in it cuts
home, which shows that human nature doesn’t
alter much from century to century. Except for
the “very small remnant’’—even that seems to
have deseited Australia’s most miserable and
justice-abandoned city.

and not necessarily a lot of capital.
# # #
Dear Bulletin,— -Members of a Brisbane
woman’s rights society call each other “old
maids” in their anger. It shows, whilst the
shrieking sisterhood pretend to despise man, how
much they redly value his judgment. The old
maid, in the general presumption, has failed to
ph ase man, therefore, “old maid ” is a term of
opprobrium. And it stings. The epithet was
hurled at an unmarried member of a female com-
mittee in Ballarat, a few years back. “ Old
maid !” was the fierce retort. “That’s all you
know !” When women fight, man begins to dis-
cover his own importance. —Yours, Snell.
The secret of N.S.W. Presbyterian
Moderator Maolnnes’s courage in attacking “the
old standards of Presbyterian faith ”in his address
denying the verbal inspiration of the Bible, is
disclosed when we learn that he was attired in
effective Court dress, consisting—
Of a.fine hlack clotti clerical coat, trimmed with frogs
of fine braid and ruffles of cream lace on the breast and
wrists, a cassock and knee breeches of the same hi ick
cloth, silk stockings, and shoes with silver buckles.
Braw feathers mak’ braw birds, and no wonder
the Moderator swelled till he exploded. Where’s
the ghost of John Knox ?

According to penitent-brewer Charrington,
the shop-assistants of Sydney work longer hours
than those of any city in Great Britain. Yes;
“shop-assistant” is locally synonymous with
“ slave ”; a male shop-assistant is, from the view-
point of industrial sociology, a slave-eunuch.

Times, has inven-

the law can get
level wi th suicides.
When any person’s
remains are con-
victed of self-mur-
der, the Times
proposes that all
property left by
the deceased
should be confis-
cated, so that his
widow and chil-
dren will be left
to starve. The
law won’t exactly

is almost as good.
If there is no pro-
perty to be con-

good effect might
be produced by

cide’s wife and
daughters, but
this is a matter
for after-conside-
ration.

A remarkable sample of justice, according
to the honorary J.P., is reported from Melbourne.
Late one night, a desire to kill somebody
possession of a gentleman afflicted with ' jim-
jams.” Accordingly, he burst into the house of
Arthur Turner, and rushed at that gentleman
with a razor. Turner got out of bed and fled
round the room, just in time to save himself from
being disembowelled, and after an active scrim-
mage he floored the invading maniac with an iron
bar, that being the first thing which came handy.
Then the police took the drunken gentleman to
the hospital and ran the other in, arid the Carlton
J.’sP. solemnly lectured him on the sin of violently
attacking the inebriate who ran amok at him in
the night, and fined him ss. for assault.
* • f
This decision should just about finish the
cheap and nasty J.P. as an administrator of
justice. If it is absolutely necessary, in a
corrupt community, that there should be some
low-priced distinction with which the local poli-
tician can reward his supporters, they should
either be made J.’sP. without any power to sit on
the bench and publicly expose their incapacity, or
some new' fragment: of the alphabet ought to be
fixed up. The only thing that is really wanted is
the right to trail sundry letters after the un-
distinguished cognomen of John Jones, and if
that person was made an lOU it would be just
as satisfactory. Also, be might be made P.N. or
N.S.F., which would do equally well, or he
might simply be created XYZ, which should be
a satisfying distinction for any small potentate.
Anyhow, the J.P. business is a failure.

A northern Chinaman was recently charged
with severely beating his European wife the day
after he had married her. John explained mat-
ters thus :—“ That one welly nice till marry and
then row, row, altogether hell!”
Sweet is the voice of the virgin, and soft as the coo of the
dove,
Daintily lisp the full lips in the delicate language of love;
But, when she tastes o’ the wedding-cake and knows that
the knot is tied, . ,
Like to a tiger that tasteth the blood of his prey is the
blooming bride.

The Salvationists of Malvern (Vic.)
announce a “ Great Thanksgiving and Harvest
Festival ” and call for contributions. Also, they
append the following list of offerings which will
be acceptable, from which it would appear that
the Army is as omnivorous as of old :
Apples, apricots, arrowroot, blue, beans, beetroot,
bananas, buns, beef (salt or fresh), bread, barley, baskets
(all sorts), billies (tin), baking powders, basins (pot, or
china), bacon, carrots, celery, cucumbers, cauliflowers,
cakes (all sorts, plain or fancy), cabbages, cocoa, car-
raway seeds, currants, currant bread, candy (sugar),
coffee, cheese. DONATIONS, large or small-dolls,
ducks, eucalyptus, eggs, fowls, fish, figs, fruit (all sorts),
gold, geese, ganders, grapes, gooseberry, greenstuff (all
descriptions), ginger beer, honey, horses, harness, houses,
hens, hams, isinglass, ink, jam (all sorts), jellies, keep-
sakes, lemons, limes, linnets (birds), MONEY, large or
small, gold, silver, copper, maize, mustard, millet, milk
(fresh or condensed), neatsfoot oil, oranges, onions, opals,
pegs (clothes , pearls, peas, pancakes, pickles, pies,
perches, pepper, pineapples, plums, pears, quinces,
queen cakes, rabbits, raisins, rhubarb, radishes, starch,
sugar, strawberry, soap, sardines, soda, salmon, tea,
tomatoes, trotters, tripe, treacle, tapioca, umbrellas,
vinegar, vases, water-melons, walnuts, yeast, yams, zebras.
This list covers pretty nearly everything except
ships, boots and dried shark, but doubtless they
will also be thankfully received. The Army
“ thankfully receives ” almost everything.

A new idea, anyhow ; “ Dear Bulletin, —
Has it ever struck you that it is the advocates of
women’s suffrage who know least about women ?
To the average male is turned a conventional
mask often worn with such consummate hypocrisy
as to deceive the wearer. There is only one class
which has a sufficiently intimate acquaintance
with female nature to impartially judge its in-
finite frailties. I want women’s suffrage investi-
gated by a jury of thoroughly-qualified rakes.—
Yours, R.D.”

Of the 3771 confinements in N.S. W.
Benevolent Asylum during the last 10 years, only
40 cases terminated fatally. Considering the con-
dition in which many women are admitted these
figures are remarkable.
• * *
A Victorian farmer lately went to Mel-
bourne for a razzle-dazzle. Proceedings began
with the usual drunk and the usual female who is
always “ laying for ” the bnsh-dazzler in his festive
hours. Then there was the wonted interval of
oblivion, after which the patient woke up penni-
less and contrite. But he also woke with a faint,
far-away recollection of some shadowy circum-
stance that seemed to loom up like a pinnacle from
a dead waste of decomposed beer-memories, and
when he had time to sort out his thoughts this
circumstance became clear to him. It his obfus-
cated state he bad married the female above-
ra ntioned. The record of the marriage was found
in the books of a certain local parson, but though
the lady’s name, or the name site used, is
there, she can’t be identified. Therefore, the
sobered buslunan has only the knowledge
that he owns a wife somewhere, whom he
doesn’t know from Adam, and even if the
lady, or someone who professes to be her,
turns up, he won’t be able to decide whether she is
the right lady or not, so that if he acknowledges
her he may get into divorce difficulties with a
previous husband, and if he doesn’t acknowledge
her he may be ultimately gaoled for desertion or
non-payment of maintenance money. All which
goes to prove that both parties to a marriage
ought to be arrested and put in solitary confine-
ment —either with or without chains—for seven
days previous to the ceremony, so that if either
of them happens to be drunk, he, or she, may
have a fair chance of sobering up prior to the
ceremony. Then, if the seven days’ probation
finds them both sober and still of the same mind,
they could have a warm bath, and have their hair
fixed up properly by the State, and be married
by the gaol-chaplain, with the governor of the
prison and a policemen and two female warders
for witnesses, and be sent on their way rejoicing.
A soldier, who served with the British
army during the Indian mutiny, has published
the story of that gory episode from a new stand-
point, and his book is making a sensation in
England. The following is an extract from a
review :
One hundred and eighteen women and 92 children were
hacked to pieces (at Cawnpore), and the sight of these
as they lay imperfectly buried in the well must have been
a maddening one; but we do not so well know how this
massacre was revenged. After the revolting deed General
Neil issued an order to the following effect: —“ After trial
and condemnation, all prisoners found guilty of having
taken part in the slaughter are to be taken into the
slaughter-house by the scavengers - men of the lowest-
cast es—and there made to crouch down and lick clean a
square foot of the blood-soaked floor.” The floor was
first damped, and if the miserable wretches refused to do
it, they were flogged into obedience. The square foot
being cleaned up in this way, they were then hanged.
A'd the general has justified his conduct by writing:
“No one who has witnessed the scenes of murder,
mutilation and massacre can ever listen to the word
‘ mercy ’ as applicable to these fiends.” On another
page we are told of one who was smeared over
with pigs’ fat, flogged by sweepers, and then hanged ;
and it seems to have been a common thing to
make prisoners break caste by forcing pork upon them,
if not by the tribunals, at any rate by the ranks. It was,
in fact, a war of downright butchery, each side seeming
to outvie the other in meroilessness. Many a time were
natives smothered in smoke fumes. As a rule, the natives;
caught were tried by officers accompanying the army,
and the trial often became a parody on justice and a
protraction of cruelty. It is only fair to say that Sir
Colin disapproved of much that was done, and
so did Lord Canning, who was nicknamed in
consequence by the English “Clemency” Canning.
When Lucknow was taken the loot was incredible. A
lieutenant got a crescent and star ornament which was
said to have sold in London for £BO,OOO, and in a week the
prize agents got over a million and a-quarter of English
money. The plunder was supposed to be divided among
those who served in the.xelief and capture of the city, but
many are known to have had far more than their share,
for while each private got something like 505., there were
many higher in the ranks who were able to redeem mort-
gaged estates, and to purchase fishing grounds and
shooting boxes. One instance is given of an estate being
cleared of a mortgage of £lBO,OOO within two years of the
mutiny, and it is inferred that loot did it. Soldiers^went
mad with pillage and got “drunk with plunder.”

Professor Thulinius.—My Dear Sir,—l feel I should be want-
ing in duty, did I neglect the opportunity of sending you my
testimony as to the wondrous efficacy of your rheumatic
specific. For the past ten years I have been a complete
martyr to rheumatism in all its forms. I have tried doctors
. . . . with a view of effecting a cure for this most trying
disease. .... I am well-known, and scores can testify
that at best I could only crawl about with difficulty. . . .
After leaving Little Bay Hospital at the close of last year I
was persuaded to try a bottle of your “ Thulinius,” and in a
week or so afterwards I could get about fairly well. Upon
again suffering a relapse last February, I took another bottle,
with an equally successful result; but at last 1 determined to
go through a course of treatment, and 1 have just completed
my fourth bottle, with the gratifying knowledge that I am
quite free from pain, can walk any reasonable distance with-
out fatigue, have an excellent appetite, and feel myself again
competent to re-enter my profession, added to which my oft-
repeated attacks of pleurisy have become a thing of the past.
I have not dieted myself in any way, so all the credit is due to
your wonderful remedy, under Providence, that my blood is
becoming purer daily* and its effect upon my liver and kid-
neys most pronounced. ... I shall only be too glad
personally to convince fellow-sufferers of the benefits to be
derived from a course of your treatment, as exemplified in
my own case, after ten years’ martyrdom.—l remain, yours
faithfully, W. H. NEWTON NEWMAN (Teacher of Elocution
and twenty-five years Actor and Manager)
[The Rev. J. D. Langley, St. Philip’s Parsonage, and Mr, H. H.
Lusk, Barrister-at-Law, can testify as to the crippled state of
Mr. Newman prior to his taking “ Thulinius.”]
