SPORTING NOTIONS.
Poor Paddy M‘Gowan will be remembered by
all who saw his finishes for the Caulfield and Australian
Cup when he had Vengeance under him. On each occa-
sion he got the Ballarat horse home by a nose by sheer
determination and the strictest attention to business. At
Caulfield he had to chase Loyalstone all down the straight
and the difference between Paddy riding Vengeance, and
James Hayes walloping away at Loyalstone, with his
excited head turning round to see himself being over-
taken, was a terrible sight for the backers of Loyalstone.
Poor little Paddy never looked over his shoulder when a
result was trembling in the balance. Which reminds one
that the blind eye spared him a good deal of temptation.
• * •
Jookey-slaughter cannot be treated with in-
difference. There will be a re-action against the coolness
with which it is surveyed.
• • *
They have threepenny bookmakers in Melbourne
now—individuals who look like Joe Thompson viewed
through the wrong end of a telescope. One of the most
enterprising of them is said to have started with a capital
of a halfpenny.
• * •
’Twill come. M'Gowan, killed on-the flat the
other day, gave up steepling, because it was so dangerous.
* * *
Jockey M'Gowan left .£3ooo—about the same
as Mick O’Brien.
The Maoriland cricketers having shown such
good form, the next eleven should be Australasian— not
Australian.
« • *
The stocky, nuggety man is at a disadvantage
in glove contests, for he cannot close and wrestle as he
could under the old P.R. rules. The case of Mace will
serve as an illustration. In his very best days there were
cleverer men with the gloves, who would not dare to face
him in the ring. For a pugilist, Mace was a very good
wrestler, and it was to this as much as anything else
that his victories over giants like King and Hurst were
due. Modern glove affairs demand less than half the
skill and courage of the old P.R.
* * #
J. E. Snell, who broke Frank Toms’ overland
cycling record, intends to try and bust his own record
again. Snell is a model cyclist, scales list. 51b., with not
an oz. of fat, and has not ruined his constitution by com-
peting in fast races. Champions like Dick Davis, Tom
Busst, and Turner would be nowhere on a long, rough
trip. Similarly, Toms, who beat Scott’s record, is a
second-rater in an Austral wheel race.
# # *
E. Jones, the lightning S.A. bowler, would be
worth his place in an Australian Eleven for fielding alone.
In the outfield for S.A. against Vic. he performed wonder-
fully.
# * *
J. Reedman, who made 113 for S.A. against
Vic., showed the best cricket of the match. His cutting
was almost a revelation, and reminds one of Percy
M'Donnell—where, by the way, is Percy M‘Donnell, now?
Reedman is a teetotaller, and a good all-round athlete,
and would have immensely strengthened the last
Australian Eleven.
* * *
Cripps, the owner and trainer of Tarcoola, told
his friends to back his horse for the Australian Cup,
saying that it was quite as well as when it won the Mel-
bourne Cup. Allowing that Carnage was not thoroughly
equal to a fast 2-mile race in November last, there is still
no accounting for the defeat of Jeweller and Portsea by
Tarcoola.
m * *
James Scobie, the hurdle and steeple artist, was
on the back of Titan for the Newmarket Handicap. No-
body seems to remember Scobie’s riding in any other flat-
race at Flemington for years. He was, presumably, acting

as shepherd to Donald Wallace's real fancy—Qaillardla.
Those people who make notes of strange circumstances
had a bit on the mare when they saw good-looking “Jim”
going out of his way to engineer the stable companion.
* * •
Australian horse-worship, as distinguished from
love of betting, was shown when the “ time ” for Portsea's
Champion Race appeared on the board. Those who won
their money had applauded the hoisting of Portsea’s
number. The hoisting of the time-record was received
with cheers from many people who, though they might
have lost their wagers, oould still raise some enthusiasm
over the performance of a good horse for the horse’s sake.
• ft *
Portsea and Light Artillery raced their rivals
almost to a standstill. Patron was fairly baked at the
finish, Sailor Prince looked as any human prince would
look after galloping three miles under a broiling sun, and
the over-rated Admiral (for whom the Prince was sup-
posed to be “ making a pace”) was gasping and trembling
long after his return to the paddock. As for Hova, the
leggy flyer who hadn’t been trained or tried to run the
Champion course—well, they had to fetch the doctor to
Hova. Mr. Sep. Miller suggested that the horse’s heart
was broken. Only two days back Hova had followed up
his Newmarket victory by winning a seven-furlong race
with scarcely an effort. Yet in the Champion this unfor-
tunate dandy had been last all the way. Hales made one
attempt to get near the front, but the others were going
too fast for the tiring sprinter.
• • •
Jones (S. A.) emerges as the bowler of the fag-
end of a century. Although last year his prestige was
not what it is now, there were knowing ones who urged
his inclusion in the All-Australia Eleven. Murdoch had
spotted him.
* * *
A miserable field in the Champion ! The V.R.O.
will be inclined to knock it out altogether, for indeed this
race, on the whole, atfter a long trial, proves a failure.
The idea used to be to galvanise up the Derby, Champion,
or some weight tor age event, to oust Melb. Cup, on the
precedent of the Epsom Derby, but it rather appears that
England will follow Australian precedent, and make its
crack event one of the big handicaps.
* » •
Quigley’s conquering career has received a cheek.
Tut Ryan knocked him out in ten rounds at Cumberland
Athletic Club, Melb.
# » «
Such big things have lately been done by the
little’uns that The Bulletin would not be surprised to
see Melb. Cup won before long by a galloway.
* 9 m
John Roberts has successfully given Mitchell
9000 in 24,000 at spot-barred billiards. Mitchell had
much the best of the game up to the close of the second
evening, but when he came to the scratch on the third he
quite disgusted the spectators. Roberts, by way of keep-
ing the company in good humour, played as even he has
seldom played in his life. At latest he was engaged in a
match for money at the same odds with Peall.
• * *
Altogether 1126 runs were made in the Vic.-
S.A. match in Adelaide, so that bowlers’ averages suffered
except O. M’Leod’s, who is probably now the best bowler
in Australia on a billiard-table wicket. By defeating
Victoria, S.A. holds the Sheffield Shield, and, taken all
round, that province’s cricket was about the best this
season. The great Giffen got going in nearly every
innings in the four matches and has an average of 75,
Moses being next with 42. Against Victoria last week
Jones, the snowy-haired wonder, clean bowled six wickets
in the last innings and thus won the match, taking three
wickets in four balls. Had S.A. had Jarvis behind the
sticks she would have won very easily. Each team had a
terrible tail, the last five S.A. batsmen being worth about
10 runs among them, and the Vies, were much the same.
Jack Harry was the best Vic. batsman, but S.A. honors
were divided among Giffen, Reedman, Lyons and Dar-
ling. Had Victoria defeated S.A. the Sheffield Shield
would have gone to N.S.W. on the basis of averages, an
absurd system which the two former provinces will oppose
next season. The best way is that in the event of a tie
next year S.A. should continue to hold the shield,
N.S.W. bowlers got splendid averages on wet wickets this
season, but surely that does not alone entitle that province
to superiority ; yet it turns the balance if the averages are
reckoned. By-the-way, the shield will be finished in a
month’s time, and will probably be placed in the Adelaide
Oval.

* * *
What a time George Hill and “Bill” Long
must have had in Melbourne I It looked as if they were
going to “suffer a recovery” on the last race of the
meeting, the Farewell Handicap, as they backed their
filly, Salutation, heavily at twelves and fourteans, and
the sensational Titan only beat her by a long neck.
# m *
“Bill ” Long, sen., had an absolute horror of
horse-racing. Since the old gentleman’s death, however,
“Bill,” jun., has made up for his enforced absence from
the sport he loves.
• # *
The cable tells us Britisher Billiard-Champion
Roberts, junr., defeated Australian Memmott by 600
points in a game of 20,000. And then it goes on to state,
as if by afterthought, that Roberts conceded the trifling
start of half the game.
* # •
Pyrmonter (Sydney) Quigley was “knocked
out ” on Saturday night in Melbourne by “Tut” Ryan.
Quigley’s nose was flattened, and his best girl will hardly
know him when he returns to his native quarries.
# * #
Titan and Parker, an uncertain pair, proved a
good combination at the V-B.C. Meeting on Saturday,
winning the All Aged Stakes and the Farewell Handicap
for Mr. D. S. Wallace.
# # #
A cricket statist makes out Grace’s average for
the last 20 years to be 41'04. W.G. has completed his
thirtieth year in first-class cricket.

A member of the recent Bible Christian
Conference at Adelaide was stricken dumb whilst
attending a sitting of that body. A few more
such visitations, and even jos. symes may admit
there is a God.
Mr. D. A. Yindin, well-known in Sydney
as a stamp-collector, has opened, or will shortly
open, a shop in the Strand, London; he will also
publish a Stamp Trade Journal. The philatelic
mania is pretty strong, but owing to bad times
such large prices cannot be obtained now as three
years ago.
The people of Pondoland are now to be
robbed and slaughtered by Rhodes & Co. “The
curtain falls on the last scene of the partition of
Africa, and the British investor, like a wallowing
rhinoceros, thrusts up his ensanguined snout from
out the steaming pool of blood and snorts out
praise to God.— Worker.
The aboriginal name of a certain N.S.W.
township, as given by Judge Windeyer, was
Mungadalla-ringadalla-lalla-lalla-lat-lat, and some-
one writes inS.M. Herald that this “ recalls the
sound of rippling water.!’ It also recalls to any-
body who has seen an English hanging the solemn
reading of the burial-service, the ringing of the
gaol-bell, and the clatter of the falling gallows-
trap. You can imagine anything.
Lord Bonington, one of Hingland’s serious
minded noblemen, doles out every year a few lbs.
of coal to each of the poor tenants on his estates.
These little donations are not altogether free, see-
ing that each poor tenant is requested, by a card
attached to his coals, to “Pray for the soul of
Edith Maud, Countess of Loudoun,” &o. His
lordship evidently fears that Edith has got into
trouble, and the fuel is meant to excite sympathy.
As soon as the poor tenant has worked up a
blazing fire, he thinks of the late Countess and
kneels down to pray.
Dear Bulletin, —Edinburgh is no longer a
British subject ! What of that? He’s more than
one subject elsewhere. Thus William Cullen
Bryant, in Indian Miscellany, 1877 : “On his
late visit to Canada, Prince Alfred spent a day
among the Mohawks of Grand River, and was
complimented by being chosen to a chieftainship
second in rank to that of Tekarihogea, the principal
sachem. ... In the event of war, his royal
highness will be the lieutenant of Tekarihogea,
and in council he pledged the honour of a prince,
that he would be found at the side of that chief
when the summons came.” Whoop! What a
suitable present a profuse scalp-lock would be to
brother Tummy! —Greg Doyle.
R.C. Archbishop John Joseph Lynch, of
Toronto, Canada:—
I have been often pained and astonished at the frequent
appeals of editors and proprietors of newspapers to their
subscribers, urging them to pay their just debts.
Cal holies, at least, cannot be unaware of their obligations
in this matter, and that absolution to a penitent heartily
sorry for his sins does not free him from the obligation of
paying his debts. The atonement for oblivion of justice
in this world will certainly be exacted in the next. The
editors and proprietors of newspapers, on their part, give
their time, the product of a high education and experi-
ence, together with their money for stationery, printing,
and wages to employees, and they expect and should
have, in common justice, a return often by no means
adequate to their outlay. A man who will not pay for a
paper he subscribed for, read, and whose contents ho
enjoyed, is a retainer of another man’s goods.
Quite right. Hell is the place where defaulting
subscribers go; also people who borrow news-
papers instead of buying them.
Dear Bulletin, — In reply to “Jerry.”
To the late Capt. “Bobby” Towns belongs the
honour of introducing the first Kanakas to
Queensland. The first labour vessel was the King
Oscar, Capt. Gibbons, and the historical Ross
Lewin was supercargo. The. Kanakas were in-
tended for sugar and cotton plantations on the
‘Logan River. Their advent on the Barcoo was
some years later. In 1874, Capt. Mackay, the
.unrequited discoverer of the Q. sugar town-site of
the same name, came to Brisbane with a long-
keeled English brig and took a cargo of returns
from Capt. Hope’s plantation of Clanland.
Ignorant of the recruiting grounds of his prede-
cessors in the trade, and conversant with any
group from Chili to Japan, he some months after-
wards returned to Rockhampton with a ship-load
of light copper-coloured, stalwart men and long-
haired maidens, most of whom went to the
Barcoo. It was rumoured that they came from
some group bordering on the China seas, and some
years afterwards the Government had considerable
difficulty in finding ships to take them back,—•
Yours, Pazzareo.
One of woman’s great ambitions is to be
between 20 and 25 years of age; when once she
lets go her hold of 25 she is desperate, and doesn’t
mind what age she becomes. This leads to curious
results in the census, as revealed by some British
figures. For instance, one census will show that
there are in the country, perhaps a million girls
between 10 and 15 years. If they all survived,
next census, taken ten years later, would show a
million women aged between 20 and 25, but al-
lowing for deaths there should not be more than
900,000. Yet it often happens that there are
actually more than a million, from which it would
appear that the girl between 15 and 20 never dies
but rather increases in number. This discrepancy,
however, is steadily diminishing; as education
advances, a woman ceases to care what age she is.
Education does more to suppress personal vanity
than anything under the sun. When physiology
is taught in schools, and children become ac-
quainted, at an early age, with horrible diagrams
of portions of themselves which look like exag-
gerated ox-livers, a good deal of the poetry goes
out of life. It is questionable if a woman whoknew
anatomy thoroughly could take much interest in
her own age. The ox-liver aspect of things would
always be with her, more or less.

A. prisoner is acquitted in Germany when
the jury’s vote is six to six. Seven to five leaves
the court to decide. Eight to four is necessary for
a verdict of conviction.
Afc Watson’s Paragon Hotel, Sydney, there
is a stable-stall laid down with “cork paving”
—a composition claimed to be superior to all
other paving for the purpose for which it is used.
It is extremely durable, soft for horses to stand
upon by reason of its elasticity; it is also non-
absorbent and inodorous. That it makes a dry,
warm, and sanitary flooring has been proved by
its use for three years. If horse-owners would let
their horses have something comfortable to rest
upon after a heavy day, hard-worked animals
would last years longer than they do now. While
standing on a hard pavement horses soon puff
their legs by stamping to get rid of flies.
Dear Bulletin. — A few details of the
inner workings of the Surveys conducted under
Melb. Metropolitan Board of Works. The jobs
are contracted for, and certain regulations and re-
strictions are hedged around the successful ten-
derer to ensure proper performance of the work
and fair treatment of his employes. One of
these rules is, that no work is to be sub-let, and
that all wages must be paid at least oncea fortnight.
How are they observed ? There has, I believe,
been hardly a single block upon which fully one-
third of the work was not sub let, and on one
I took some work the profits of which had filtered
through two hands ere they reached yours truly ;
and as for the latter bye-law, I know few firms of
surveyors who carry out its provisions. One
contractor, on being importuned by a man to whom
he owed over .£2O, offered him two shillings on
account, another to whom I was holding out for a
large payment and instancing my liabilities, cut
me short with “That nothing, one man’s got the
bailiffs in. ” On the job where I was last employed
I got thirty shillings a week, sketching and mea-
suring, and had sometimes to wait as Jong as eight
weeks for my money ; on no occasion was my
salary less than a month in arrear. I had with
me a chainman and a boy. The former, a mar-
ried man with several children, received the
princely remuneration of 15s. a week, while 4s.
(a penny an hour) sufficed for the nipper. The
salaries usually paid are: Instrument men, sup-
plying their own “ jigger,’’ from £2 to 50s. a week.
Sketehers and measurers from .£1 to 30s. Chain-
men, anything under the latter figure, and boys
from 14 upwards, from 3/6 to ss. The boys do,
or are supposed to do, the work formerly done by
men at 6s. per day, but the youngsters are not
particular to an inch or two, or even a foot or two,
for that matter, in taking measurements, hence
the accuracy of the work is questionable; but
unless errors are so glaring that the Board returns
the plans to be re-done, the profits are all on the
contractor’s side and the risk is worth taking.
The highest-paid of the above are skilled men who
have, as a rule, served an apprenticeship for a term
of years, and in many instances paid a premium
for the privilege, and with all, holidays and wet
days and days absent through sickness are
vigorously docked from the pay of those on salary,
even from the boys’ 3/6, and repairs to tapes, poles,
etc., have also to be paid from the workers’ pockets.
The hours are from eight to ten, per diem, and as
the nature of the work necessitates standing the
whole of the time, the former seems quite long
enough. In addition to this there remains the
fact that towards the end of a job all hands have
in many cases a very fair chance of being “left ”
for some weeks’screw and with no earthly show
of ever getting it in full. Certainly, if sufficiently
strong representations be madeby the unfortunates,
the Board will compel the surveyor to make a
statutory declaration that all his employes have
been paid, before granting him his final certificate ;
but the said certificate is often not large enough
to meet all the outstanding amounts, so adiv. of
so many shillings in the .£ is all one gets. Couple
the above with the facts that the boss is continually
growling about lost time, slow work, etc., that
we have to answer all the silly questions of the
people whose houses we enter, and you will have
some sort of an idea what a rosy billet we have.
Contrast the action of London County Council
who rule that on all contracts under them shall
the standard wage be paid, and sees that its orders
are carried out, while the Southern corporation
simply sends an official about occasionally to yarn
with the surveyor, ask a few employes a few
questions, which they must either lie to or lose
their billets, and who returns reporting satisfac-
tion. But the former body has live men like
Rosebery and John Burns among its members,
while the Vic. one is composed entirely of subur-
ban mayors and aldermen of the type of Thomas
Bent, the furious Mangel Wurzel man from South
Brighton.—McG.

DOMESTIC ECONOMY!
WHAT IS IT ?
DOMESTIC ECONOMY is primarily the art of feeding and clothing the family at as low a rate as possible,
without impairing the family digestion, or taking away the character of its members for being well and com-
fortably dressed. Domestic economy is not comprised in buying a bad article for sixpence when better can be
got for sevenpence ; but if the ruling price for the best is eightpenoe, and somebody comes along and offers it for
sevenpence, then it’s domestic economy to close with the merchant so offering. By all means study this great
and glorious science of social life, because its due and proper observance cannot fail to make poor people rich, and
rich ones richer. What say you ? Will you do your business at

A very high official, who is understood to have

The immense Westralian concessions granted to
an English crowd—the Hampton Plains syndicate—abut
on Coolgardie, as the world knows. The syndicates claims
to own all the minerals in their territory. Should rich
gold be found within the grant (which is more than likely)
and thousands of miners rash the place, which would ha
a dead certainty, we shall see—what we shall see. Thera
is no force in Australia that can deal with 10,000 men of the
sort who go gold-digging. The Hampton Plains syndicated
may have to whistle for “ their” gold.

There are now several Lords gold-digging in

# • •
David Lindsay, back at Coolgardie from the
new rush, brought three nuggets—62 oz., 52 oz. and 30 oz.
—water, he reports, was retailed at 2s. per gal. (it after-
wards rose to 55.); flour, 2s. per lb. Between the rush
and Coolgardie is a waterless 50 miles; 6000 oz. gold
(another account says 2000 oz.) found to date. Field rich,
but patchy.
» * *
Privileges enjoyed by prospectors at the new
Coolgardie rush:—Clements, crawling on his hands and
knees, too exhausted to walk, was the first to return,
with several ounces in his pouch, and glorious news of the
rich find on his lips. Two days of semi-delirium followed,
during which he was spoon-fed in the first camp he
struck. Massey’s party next appeared, represented by
one who said ail four camels on which they’had travelled
were forty miles distant, being knocked up after ten days
without water. During the two days employed in this
P6scue, Jones, the pedestrian, was wandering in the scrub,
subsisting’on a bit of stale damper, blood falling from hig
nostrils. In his direst extremity, when stark naked, he
was staggering, almost a maniac, under the burning sun,
he struck the camel-pad and found a billycan of water
left by the rescue patty for some of their comrades in the
rear.

parative merits of D.T.’s and never-touch-its in an
Arizona (U.S.A.) raiiway-oaiyiage. The dispute waxed

sprawled all over the carriage, without taking any part in

boys, I’m no jedge. Some" say it’s a good thing ; some
say it’s a bad thing. 1 dunno. I never tasted it." What
price that man as a W.A. prospector?—Yours, Austra-
lian.

Al>out the end of May is plenty of time for people to
think of going there, unless each man has £lOO behind
him. It may rain at Coolgardie to-morrow, but it may
also not rain thereuntil June. By the way, judging by the
almost entire absence of birds, animals, and, conse-
quently, of natives, I believe the average rainfall at Cool-
gardie must be nearer 7in. than 14in.—C.D. Perth, 9th
Feb., 1894.

A quarter of an inch of rain has fallen in the
Coolgardie district. Estimated that 2000 oz. picked up at

Accounts from the Wyalong goldfield over-
shadow all other N.S.W. mining news, though it has only
sprang into prominence wiihin the past fortnight. Pros-
pecting has long been going on, and the new find is in an
old and favourably-known mining district, 40 miles from
'femora, 84 from Forbes and Young, and 17 from Bar-
medman. The country is mostly flat scrub, but there is
good water and cheap provisions. There are from 1000
to 2000 men on the ground, and crowds flocking.
There are said to be 40 claims on payable gold—
mostly in narrow ironstone veins, easily worked. The
best claim, Neild’s, has a quartz reef heavily charged with
pyrites. Several crushing*, totalling 100 tons, from
various claims average over 3oz. The Warden and others
consider the field permanent, and likely (given enough
water for crushing) to employ many men when machines
are on the ground.

time.

IN THE LIONS’ DEN AT FILLIS’S.
John Plumper Hoolan has resigned his
seat in Q. Parliament in favour of Labour-leader
Tom Glassey. In acquainting his constituents
with his intentions, Hoolan said—“ God has been
very good to the Labour-party by the deaths of
Burns and M‘Farlane, for a Labour-member will
step into the latter’s shoes as had one already
in the former’s.” The meeting passed a vote of
confidence in Providence.
The fourth number of Under Lock and
Key has an interesting article on the separate con-
finement of prisoners. Incidentally we learn
that the governor of one N.S.W. gaol has
excluded Shakespeare from the prison library as
“an immoral work,” And “one man now under
life sentence for murder, and who ought to have
been hanged, if ever capital punishment were
justifiable, gets the Home News regularly.”
Dear Bulletin, —Your contributor of 24th
Feb. makes a grievous error re W. R. Wilson,
whom he evidently does not know so well as does
the present writer. There are scores who, had it
not been for Mr. Wilson’s kindly advice, would
now be working hard for a living. And during
the last days of the silver boom none wrote off bad
debts with a freer hand, instead of keeping them
hanging over his debtors’ heads till better times,
than W. R. Wilson —a white man for more like
whom the world would be a lot better. — Miner,
Hillgrove (N.S.W.).
Well-rounded. By Mr. Fitchett :
On Sunday night the southern and western skies were
all aflame with the Aurora Australis. Soft crimson clouds
stretched almost from the horizon to the zenith, pulsating
with waves now of fainter and now of richer colour, and
shot through at irregular intervals with faint vibrations
of ice coloured light. It was a beautiful sight, and was
made yet more beautiful and mysterious as the spectator
thought of the huge far-off icebergs somewhere in the
direction of the South Pole which caught on their
splintered peaks and crystalline slopes the rays of a far-
off sun, broke them up into their elementary colours, and
flung them through leagues of space on to the midnight
arch of the Australian sky.
N.S. Wales Commandant Hutton, after
bravely encouraging the partially - paid forces
under his command to accept their reduced pay—
first, from =£l2 to £9 125., and then from £9 l2s.
to £6 per annum—with the “pluck” of “sol-
diers,” has given publicity to his revised list of
salary for the “ permanent ” hirelings. This list
shows an increase of salaries among five “officers,”
amounting to £395 per annum ; a new appointment
(“Mounted Brigade, 2nd in Command”) of .8700
per annum ; increases to three non-commissioned
officers of <B29 —£1 (!), £l6 and £l2; and de-
cmises to non-com.’s totalling £251. Thus Mr. Hut-
ton’s economies with respect to “permanents”
represent —increases, £8124 ; decreases, £251; or
an increase of £7873. Yet the Com. doesn’t claim
to be an Irishman.

The Hon. John A. Wallace has purchased the No. 3
crusher from the defunct Loyola mine, and it is being
placed in position on his Bethanga works and will be used
for dry crushing.
From Queensland we have received an order from the
Mount Morgan G.M. Co. for a No. 3 crusher for their new
chlorinating works, and which will doubtless lead to
others being ordered. Messrs. Sutton and Co., of Bris-
bane have also ordered a No. 1 crusher for their new
chlorinating process ; it will be used for dry crushing in
Brisbane.
The Kapai-Yermont G.M. Co. of Auckland, N.Z., have
purchased a complete plant consisting of No. 3 crusher,
copper-plate amalgamating tables, framework and steam-
engine and boiler. This plant is ready and is now being
packed for shipment.
The Valgogrin Crushing Co., N.S.W., has purchased a
complete plant, consisting of No. 2 crusher, with engine,
boiler, amalgamating tables, blanket strakes, and other
fittings for use on this field, the various companies having
combined and formed a separate company for the pur-
pose ; this plant is also being packed for shipment.
The Austin Island G.M. Co., Murchison, W.A., has pur-
chased a No. 1 mill with engine, boiler, amalgamating
tables, and blanket strakes complete. This plant has
reached Geraldton, The Otis Co. had this plant erected
complete in their yards with the necessary buildings built
over them before shipment, and despatched the whole
complete, the total weight, including the buildings, being
under 10 tons.
The Francis Reward G.M. Co., Cue, W.A., has also just
received their plant, consisting of a No. 3 mill, with
amalgamating tables and blanket strakes, engine, boiler,
and mill framing complete. The total weight of this
plant was 10J tons, and it will be at work by this time.
It will be noted from the foregoing that our opinion of
this crushing plant is being fully borne out; the orders
coming in from every part of Australia, and the numerous
inquiries for estimates show the favour with which it is
being received by the raining community.
We enclose the following information re compressed
fodder.
Compressed Fodder.— We called attention a short time
since to a new outlet for our surplus agricultural produce
—chaff, oats, maize, &c., by means of which the produce
is compressed into a bulk of only 40 cubic feet to the ton
weight. The advantages of horse-feed in this form are
obvious, the saving in freight and storage space, espe-
cially for inland and oversea carriage, being enor-
mous, independent of its preservative qualities. This
is quite apparent when it is stated that feed
for two horses for a fortnight’s journey can be
conveniently stowed away under the seat of an ordinary
Abbot buggy. The process of compression in no way
injures the food properties of the produce, and nothing
whatever is added to it. The Agricultural Department
here have had an analysis made of the samples, and states
that the nutritive value is equal to that of the uncom-
pressed fodders. Samples have been taken to England
by the Hon. R. Reid, of Victoria, with the result that he
has cabled the Agricultural Department of Victoria to have
100 tons sent to England at once, at the same time stating
that there is an excellent market tor it. Samples were
also taken to India by the Government commercial envoys
with the result that orders have been cabled for ship-
ments, so that there is a great opening for a large export
trade being opened up as soon as the necessary plants are
in working order, and it will be a great relief to our agri-
cultural friends. Wo understand a strong syndicate has
been formed to work these patents in all the Australasian
colonies, and the machinery for the first plant is now
being erected with all speed by the Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Co., who have carried out all the preliminary experi-
ments.

The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Company
has a superb stock of fish for the Lenten season,
including not only the home-caught article, but
also the best to be had in Maoriland ; while some
choice English fish are to hand by recent mail-
boat. Prices are reasonable; quality is perfect;
and fish can be packed and sent anywhere in the
city or provinces on the shortest notice.
THREE FORTUNATE WOMEN.
We will introduce you to the oldest one first. Her
name is Mrs. Jessie Russell, and she lives near the
city of New York, in America. She was born in
London in 1787, and is therefore 105 years old.
She remembers many incidents of her childhood,
and still has traces of former beauty. Her eyes
are bright, and become animated when she talks.
She retains her mental faculties completely. She
descends two flights of stairs to every meal, and
returns without assistance. Up to three years
ago she was a regular attendant at the Greenwood
Baptist Church. Her sight is excellent, and she
reads the newspapers every day, and takes great
interest in the news from England. She has
never had any serious illness in her life, and her
appetite and digestion are almost as good as they
were fifty years ago.
The second woman says; “ Ever since I was a
child I have suffered from illness. Thirteen years
ago a tired, languid, and heavy feeling came over
me. The white of my eyes became tinged with
yellow, my skin was sallow, and my hands and
feet were cold and clammy. My mouth tasted
badly, especially in the morning, and I was often
sick, vomiting a sour, frothy fluid. I had a pain
in the chest and sides, heartburn, and flatulency.
For four years I suffered like this, and I shall be
glad to recommend the medicine that finally cured
me.” (Signed) (Mrs.) Frances Emily Smith, 49a,
Wood house Street, Leeds, January 25th, 1892.
The third woman says: “ I have been delicate
all my life. For the past six years I have always
felt tired, languid, and weak. I had a poor
appetite, a bad taste in the mouth, and pain after
every morsel I ate. I was dizzy, and often felt as
if I was going to fall. I always felt sick, and
would retch and strain, but could bring nothing
up. There was a dull pain at my right side and
a dreadful pain between the shoulders. I never
knew what it was to be well. After being on my
feet for a while my legs would swell very much;
No doctor was able to help me, nor any medicine.
that is, up to the time I took the one which gave
me my health back. I never felt so well in my life
as Ido now.” (Signed) (Mrs.) Sarah Sharman,
Clinton, near Market Deeping, February 19th,
1892.
Why do we put these three women in a group—
the one venerable woman in America and the
two others here in England ? They are perfect
strangers to one another and always will be. We
do it to show how long and happily a woman may
live if she only happens to escape the malady
that everywhere threatens her sex; and also to
show that those who have been burdened and
cursed with it may be cured and once more taste
the joys of health.
Mrs. Smith concludes her letter in these words :
“After taking three bottles of Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup all pain and sickness left me, and
I knew no more of the ailment that had troubled
me so long, indigestion and dyspepsia. I know
others who have been benefited by the Syrup. All
sufferers should use it. r'
Mrs. Sharman adds that after she had doctored
and doctored and taken everything, almost with-
out getting any good from it, Mr, Webster, a
grocer of Glinton, said, “ Why don’t you use
Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup ? ” She acted on
his suggestion, and health wasn’t long in coming.
“I cannot speak too highly of Seigel’s Syrup,”
she says. We should agree with her in that
opinion, for a remedy that will in a few weeks put
an end to a case of chronic indigestion and dys-
pepsia is certainly worth a good word or two.
Now here you have these three Englishwomen
—one so fortunate as to have lived more than a
century without an illness ; the others ( still more
fortunate ) have known the sadness of suffering
and the pleasure of recovery.
Oh, my ! oh, my ! how hard it is to tell who is
best off in this queer world.

The Man Who Teamed for the New.
(FOR THE BULLETIN)
. ,
There was once a man who yearned tor
the New. He was sick of conventionalities, and
tired to death of paved roads. Ho longed for a
course untrammelled and unexplored. Ho was
absolutely nauseated with the sameness with which
people followed each other up and down through
the world, one after another, treading in each
others footsteps just like so many sheep. If here
and there, there did happen to be one who
branched away from the others and started a new
road for himself it was because he was blind,
They were all blind, but the leaders were the
blindest of all, he said.
He was so disgusted with things that be deter-
mined he would invent an altogether new system
of living, and to this end he drew out on paper,
and with infinite trouble, a plan of action which
had never before been followed by any mortal
man. I his took him several years to arrange, and
just as it appeared to him to be as near perfection
as he could hope to make it, he discovered that
one of his grandest and most original plans had
been adopted by* a man in a novel.
It was a great blow to him. It comp etely upset
his vvorknigs for he took to reading other novels,
and was confronted with the appa ling discovery
that men in novels did everything that men out of
novels did not do. . . ,
You will perceive the desperation of such a
situation. He cast about to make an end of him-
self, saying that there was nothing new under the
sun, but before he could find a way of (lying
that nobody had ever tried before, a very conven-
tioiial thing indeed happened to him. He fell in
love and forgot about the dying. _
night he bad been to see the girl he was
engaged to, but did not love, and did not mean to
m my. She was, of course, not pretty. Nor
was she better than pretty. He had taken
care not to have anyone so hackneyed as that,
But she was intense in a way that no other
woman in the land could approach, and her
eyes were goggle.
She said to him this night, “ Do you guess my
thoughts before I utter them? Do you always
know what I’m thinking of? I do with you. It
is so beautiful.”
He looked uneasy.
“ Let us take hands and sit in silence for ten
minutes/ and at the end of that time we’ll tell each
other what each of us thought of. Begin.” They
to >k hands and were silent. She looked into
space. He began to think what goggle eyes she
h id. Then he tried not to think what goggle eyes
sh ! had, and was confused with the thought that
he was thinking. that he would not think what
goggle eyes she had,
At the end of ten minutes her eyes came back
from space and fastened on his face.
“ You thought of love and death,” she said,
H >r voice thrilled.
He said nothing.
“ Tell me what you thought I thought of,” she
said.
“ Me,” he said modestly.
Sire looked hurt. “Ah, no,” she said, “not
that. I thought,” she sighed, “ I thought of two
fair twin souls meeting after a million years of
parting to kiss in space.”
He didn’t know what to say to that, so shortly
after fie went home. It was as he was going home
that the conventional thing happened to him. He
fell in love. This girl, of course, was very pretty,
and her prettiness was the only intense thing
ab “ Allow me to pass your tickets,” said he.
“ Thank you,” she said. I believe those were
the words she usedj bllt tbe i ook that went with
them, the poise of her head, the dimple at her
mouth, and the sudden curving of her wrist as she
passed them, meant more than words—meant, in
f ac t j something like this : “You’re a young man.
And how pretty I am. Look at me. lam not
going to take any notice of you, though. But be
sure you look at me. Nobody ever had such long
eyelashes as I have. And what pretty boots I
wear. Don’t forget to look at me, and if I appear
to be offended, you’ll know that I’m really
nothing of the kind.”
He interpreted all this rightly, and looked
hard at her> She b to read a book . Then
he looked oufc of the window . She looked
U p and bowed and smiled at an imaginary some-
bod a( . the end of the car . Then she droppe d her
handkerchief, and was very surprised when he
nickod ifc and it fco | le r. So the acquaint-
ance began
Yes, less than a month after that they were
enga?ed but he had not to i d her that he was not
going to marry, and that he was also engaged to
the girl with the goggle eyes. He told though
of hig lan o{ lif e , and 'she listened with great
gravifcy> Sh e was a smart girl, and had her full
s hare of brains
“Very nice,” she said. “Um ! Very nice.”
g be nodded her head,
Encouraged by her nod, he thought it would
p erbaps be a s well to let her know that marriage
was one () f the conve ntional things he had ean-
celled from his calendar. He was a little nervous
0 f beginning the subject, and his nervousness in-
creased when he saw tiny sparks flash up in the
softness of her dark eyes.
“You see,” he ended up, “they all marry, in
the novels and out, so I couldn’t possibly do it.”
She looked towards the door, and somehow—
this was always a vague phrase to him—he found
himself out in the street, unconventional at the
cost of the prettiest girl he had ever dreamt of.
# # #
For two days he was very miserable. But
he still had hope, in its commonest form—the
postman. However, she did not write, and on
the third day he learnt accidentally that she was
on the verge of becoming engaged to another
man. She was, in fact, about to adopt the best
means of self-consolation that mortals possess,
the consoling of someone else. She only waited
for the someone else to ask her. Everybody in
her street knew she was going to say “Yes.”
For two hours the young man who yearned for
the New walked up and down past her house. In
that time all the hopes of his life-time changed to
vanity. (She certainly was very vain.) Nothing
remained desirable but the girl he had lost. He
cast aside all Ids yearnings, and, marching heroic-
ally, knocked at the front door with the speech all
ready on his lips. “I come to yield. Marriage
is the most beautiful bond in life. There could
be nothing conventional in it. Other people
have married, but not such people as you and I;
you so charming and beautiful, and I so—”
There he was going to pause and sigh just long
enough for her to burst into tears, and be recon-
ciled.
What really did happen was much shorter. He
knocked at the door, and she, opening it herself,
said “I’m out,” and slammed it again in his face.
As he walked away he bought a penny paper,
in the hopes of finding some novel suggestion for a
suicide. Glancing through it, he saw a short
story, headed “ The Idiot Who was Afraid to Die,”
When he had read it he trembled.
It was the story of his own life. It betrayed,
with a cruel fidelity of detail, all the secrets of his
plan of life ; it jeered at him ; it held him up, and
begged everybody to laugh at him, tossing and
kicking against fate. It said he was always
bragging about killing himself, but never found
the time. It told the incident of his engagement
to the girl he had loved, and its subsequent end.
And its moral was that men should be warned,
and go and do otherwise.
And it was signed with the name of the girl he
loued !
He went away toward a river. He was aware
dimly that this was not altogether a new thing to
do, but he was too depressed to mind. Only once
an instinct of his old yearning cams over him.
It was just before he plunged.
“In novels,” he said, “the hero always takes
off his coat, and flings it aside. I’ll keep mine
on.”
Then he plunged, and went to find a world
where there were no novels, where men were not
sheep, where the yearning for the new was forever
satisfied. He went, in fact, to the New Jeru-
salem, which was not wholly a new thing to do,
if he had only reflected on it.
The girl declared she would almost have loved
him if he had only been a sheep.
Sydney.

tities of which are being forwarded to sufferers
throughout the colonies. It is obtainable, with
advice, from 257 George-street, Sydney.

verdict—reversed on appeal—against the British
Museum for libel, is said to be almost as hand-
some now as in the old days of her notoriety.

BAXTER’S LOAN.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It was one of those raw, blustering nights when
a man, comfortably housed, says, “ I pity those
poor devils sleeping in the park to-night,” and
pats himself on the back for his charitable feeling.
One of the “poor devils” sitting on a wet seat
in the park thought with regret of his recent past,
when he was going through his money in the
orthodox fashion. He thrust his hand into his
sodden pocket as a matter of habit, for he well
knew there was not a single coin there, then
rose, shook himself, and commenced to walk along
the asphalt path for the sake of warmth. If he
had not been so very shabby he would have
sought out some of his old haunts on the chance
of meeting an acquaintance not too proud to say,
Hello, Hardwick 1” and good for a drink, at
any rate.
The Post-office clock boomed midnight, when
he saw some one coming towards him under the
dripping trees. Hardwick had not tried begging
yet, but he determined, if the new-comer looked
well-off, to appeal to his charity. The new-comer
was a red-faced, middle-aged gentleman. He
wore a white hat, somewhat on one side, an over-
coat unbuttoned, and carried a closed umbrella.
He was chuckling and talking to himself, and
ooked very happy. To a professional dead-beat
ho would have been a merciful dispensation of
Providence, with a capital P. Hardwick ap-
proached him, and the two met and halted.
“ Hello, Charley !” said the man with the white
hat, “you out at this time? For shame!” and he
made a playful dig with his umbrella. Astonished
at this reception Hardwick commenced to stammer
a humble request for a small donation, when the
other interrupted him. “Fact is, Charley, I’m
awfully glad to meet you. Got a bit sprung, you
know, and feel like sitting down for a sleep, but
then some of those d d unemployed will go
through me. _ Now, look here, Charley ” —here he
took Hardwick’s arm—“you’re a steady young
file, just take care of these notes for me ” —ho
thrust a bundle into Hardwick’s hand—“and I’ll
sit down and have forty winks. Don’t like going
home screwed, you know.”
Hardly knowing what to do, Hardwick led the
intoxicated man to a retired seat.
“He’ll surely give me something for looking
after him,” he thought. “ Might as well know who
he is, though. What’s your address now?” he
asked.
‘ 1 Oh ! same old ticket—Tom'<Baxter, care of
Renwick and Rehwick,” and Mr. Baxter, with his
hat still more on one side, fell fast asleep as soon
as he sat down. Hardwick glanced up and down
the wet, glistening pathway, then he went to the
nearest lamp and examined the notes. In fives
and ones there was nearly .£5O. He put them in
his pocket and started doing sentry-go in front of
the sleeping man. Baxter had evidently mistaken
him for someone he knew—misled, in his stupid
condition, by a chance resemblance.
Hardwick had .£5O in his pocket, and ten
minutes before he had not a shilling. Needless to
enter into the mental conflict that ensued. When
the clock struck one he had decided to take a loan,
only a loan, of the .£5O. Baxter was evidently a
well-to-do man who could afford to “lend” the
money. Hardwick withdrew into the shadows
and watched the man on the seat. He had, doubt-
less, more money in his pocket and a gold watch
and chain. Hardwick would not leave him a prey
to the first prowler who might come along. He
had so much conscience.
In the course of another hour Baxter awoke,
sobered. He got up, felt in all his pockets, seemed
disappointed, then stood musing. Suddenly ho
appeared to remember something, for ho straight-
ened his hat, buttoned his coat, put his umbrella
up, and marched firmly away. Baxter had just
remembered that before sitting down he met
Charley Stimson, the son of his old friend, and
gave him the roll of notes to take care of. That
was all right.
Hardwick, taught a lesson by the past, made
good use of the loan, as he persisted in regarding 1
it. He avoided all his former acquaintances and
departed for Coolgardie by the first steamer.
Luck followed him ; he did well; he did better ;
at last he struck it rich.” He never forgot
Laxter. Half-a-dozen times he was on the point
of forwarding .£5O to the address which he had
written down, but something always interfered to
stop nun. His favourite dream was that he might
und Laxter in low circumstances and astonish
him by repaying the loan a hundred-fold. Before
six months had passed Hardwick was a fairly
rich man, with a large share in a good reef.
Now, Baxter was not by any means wealthy.
He was on the verge of insolvency. He might
have pulled through by the grace of old Stimson
his principal creditor, but when he said that he
met Charley Stimson (a very good young man) in
Hyde I ark, at 12 o’clock at night, and entrusted
him with .£5O, Stimson raged. Then Baxter got
obstinate, and swore that Charley must have
stuck to the money. There was a law-case and,
finally, rum for Baxter, who went down till he
could n t get any lower.
It was again a rainy night, when Hardwick,
just back from Westralia, was returning home
across the park. The pathway was deserted
as before ; the wet pools glistened in the gaslight.
A figure rose from the very seat where he once
sat. A figure in an old, white hat and shabby
greatcoat, holding a battered umbrella. The
wreck of Baxter!
Hardwick s heart stood still as the phantom
halted ami accosted him. Now was his oppor-
tunity. Take this waif home to his hotel and give
him of the best. That was the first generous
thought. Then came those cold, calculating
second thoughts. l< Don’t be in a hurry • give
him something to-night, and do the rest when
you re calm to-morrow, ”
Hardwick had a sovereign and a shilling in his
pocket. Ho would give Baxter—who plainly did
not remember him—the sovereign, and find out
his address next morning. He thrust the coin in
the man s hand and went on. Arrived at the
hotel he went to the bar before going upstairs ; he
pulled out the remaining coin to pay for his drink
! He had given Baxter the shilling.
Never mind, I will make it up to-morrow,” he
thought as he dropped off to sleep.
Next morning, in a virtuous frame of mind
Hardwick hunted up Baxter’s address. Poor
Baxter had no address, but lie still called occasion-
ally at his old firm’s offices in the hope of some-
thing turning up. Hardwick smiled as lie thoucrht
of what would turn up next time Baxter called.
He went home and sat down with his pleasant
anticipation. He could write a cheque for four
figures, if he liked, but that would be too much
all at once. Baxter had evidently gone to the
dogs; he must be reclaimed gradually—say ,£2O
for a start. He would send it to-morrow; mean-
time, Baxter would be all right—he had the
sovereign to go on with.
In the middle of the night Hardwick woke,
and one of those unpleasant thoughts which always
nit a fellow at that time reminded him that it
was a shilling he had given Baxter, not the
sovereign he intended.
, At tiie same time, down in hell, the devil re-
minded the paving-commissioners that many
streets needed repairing. Next day was Sunday,
so Hardwick could do nothing.
On Monday, however, he took his cheque-book,
and seriously considered the subject. He deter-
mined to consult lien wick and Renwick, telling
them some plausible tale to account for his
interest m Baxter. Putting his cheque-book in
h-Pocket, no picked up the morning paper,
I ho first thing he saw was a paragraph stating
that the_ dead body of a man had been found
boating m Woolloomooloo Bay, and that the
deceased had been identified as Thomas Baxter,
once well-known in city circles.
X suppose, muttered Hardwick, after staring
very hard at the wall, “he would only have spent
lfc i!l drink. _ Perhaps it’s all for the best.”
I hen, as it was a fine morning, he went for a
stroll m the park.
DELCOMRN.
The Mint of God.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Would God but still the clangour of the sky
And vyheels of destiny that harshly roll,
Crushing the golden glory of the soul
From out the dross of dull mortality.
Then, every doubting heart and straining eye
Might see and understand the perfect whole,
The earth, the stars, and spiritual goal,
Obedient to the Master standing by.
But while we blindly drive from dust to dust,
Confounded in the chaos of an hour,
With death and hell tumultuous everywhere.
We cannot hide our hearts in trembling trust,
Or differ from the dying daisy flower,
Save in this weary wisdom of despair.
Parramatta, N.S.W.

In the Basin Reserve.
(A Love Story.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
They sat on a seat placed beneath the shadow of
the trees in the Basin Reserve.
Just a flower that wanted culling—just a
creature born to be loved—just a being of the most
exquisite sensibility (with apologies to the memory
of Mahomet) —just the ideal creation (or creative)
of the amorous fancy—the prolonger of life—the
sharer of joys—in fact, a woman, not a piano—all
that she was.
They sat, and sat, and sat on.
And as they sat, a great strange wonder (with
apologies to Rider Haggard)grew upon them, i.e.,
thrust itself palpably before them.
The rain had left a little pool at her feet, and a
spider had fallen into the water. The man leaned
forward and quietly extricated it, thereby saving
its life.
Ah ! she thought, the man who will save the
life of a spider will not be cruel to a woman, brute
though he appears.
Two souls that stroked as one.
She died of a kick administered by a boot on
her husband’s leg.
Against her judgment, not deceived, etc. (with
apologies to Milton), but foully overcome by hob-
nails.
His goal was the gaol (with apologies to the
Australian football clubs).
Maoriland.

Harvard College, one centre of the Free-
trade cult in the U.S., has been struck heavily by
the awful depression which followed the advent
of the Democratic party, and a whole gang of
professors have been retrenched. Says the
Argonaut:
-TUi s is tiie chain that leads up to the college professor:
the Democratic threats of Freetrade tariff-tinkering scared
the importers, and they stopped importing ; it scared the
merchants, and they stopped buying ; this left the manu-
facturers without orders, and they shut down the factories
and the mills; this left the mill-hands without any money
and they stopped buying things from the shop-keepers •
the shop-keepers stopped buying things from (he mer-
chants; the railroads had nothing to haul from mill to
merchant, from merchant to shop-keeper; the railroads
began losing money, although they discharged men, cut
wages, and laid off trains ; they failed to earn their fixed
charges and operating expenses ; they failed to pay the
interest on their bonds; Harvard College has money
invested in railroad bonds ; the railroads are in the hands
of receivers, and cannot pay interest; Harvard gets no
interest on her investments ; her revenues are reduced •
she is forced to discharge the professors who taught the
free trade that shut the shops that stopped the mills that
destroyed the freight traffic that ruined the railroads that
cut off the interest that paid the professors their salaries.

Gen. Teesdale, formerly equerry to the
Prince of Wales, bequeathed a couple of Gustave
Dora’s pictures to his royal idol, and left instruc-
tions that all his private correspondence relating
to Albert Edward aforesaid was to be religiously
destroyed. Had he left his bundles of letters to
the nation they might have helped to hasten the
inevitable.
Stead’s so-called cure for drunkenness is
the old, old “vinegar of quassia,” known to our
great-great-great-grandfathers, who, however,
didn’t want to be cured of drunkenness. But
‘‘ vinegar of quassia ” won’t cure chronic, or even
intermittent, dipsomania. It is a mild reliever of
fevers malarial; and many will remember the
wooden cups, like wineglasses, carved out in quassia
wood ; you filled them with water and let them
stand a minute. The medicinal virtues of quassia
being soluble in cold water, presently you get an
infusion of quassia—supposed to be prophylactic
against malarial attacks. Stead must either have
unchainable impudence or unfathomable ignorance
to attempt to palm off “ vinegar of quassia ” as a
new cure for alcoholism.
Discussing the chance of hereditary strength
versus hereditary brains, London Referee says :
After a hard battle for life the Shakespeare blood sur-
vived on the female side, and has been traced through
nine generations—l allude to the Hart branch of the
family, the last known member of which was George Hart,
of Birmingham, a plumber, who emigrated to Australia
about 30 years ago. Has there been a second genius in
that long line ? Not at all. The Harts have been a race
of utter mediocrities, plying the humblest callings and for
the most part miserably poor. Yet longevity in the Hart
family occurs over and over again.
The Bulletin will maintain that the Hart who
came to Australia must have been a genius, for
every plumber is a genius in his way. And if
this same George Hart started plumbing over here
he ought to be anything but poor by this time.
No plumber can remain miserably poor after
getting his first job, if he follows the plan of hang-
ing on to each job until he gets another.
From Bathurst (N.S.W.) Advocate, which
thinks Mozart, Handel, and all the other music
of the spheres nothing to the melody of a cat-of-
nine-tails playing on a man’s raw back
To call such a fellow as this a man is an insult to the
species. To describe such a fellow as he, who would do
as he did—smear the exterior house fixings of an enemy,
real or imaginary, with the contents of a cesspit, as brute,
as beast, as a thing wiih the mind of a chimpanzee and
the foul instincts of a buzzard, would be to offer to each
of those creatures an unmerited insult, for God made
them so. Patrick Rooney is a grown human being, not a
monkey. Patrick Rooney is a fellow man, not a buzzard.
Patrick Rooney is of the species which the clergy tell us
is made in the image of the Creator. Yet Patrick Rooney
is meaner than the lowest of the creatures we have
named. Never preach the abolition of the cat-o’-nine-
tails to the Advocate.

died. Had she lived to be a woman, she might
have entered the W.C.T.U. and taken to the
platform.

A Bush Wedding.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“He cometh not,” she said.
She was the bride. Thirty years of hard work
had not made her look younger ; but her brideship
clothed her with a certain indefinable attraction,
and—who ever dared call a bride ugly ?
She stood at the window, her rather snub nose
pressed against the glass, her eyes striving to
pierce the veil of rain and mist that obscured the
track leading along the side of the range down
which the laggard groom would have to come.
The view was cheerless. Paddocks overgrown
with bracken stretched as far as the eye could
reach, until they met the wall of scrub; a wet,
miserable cow endeavoured to shelter her scraggy
frame on the lee-side of the trunk of a dead gum,
whose sap, churned by the rain, lay in frothy
heaps about the roots. The dripping tree-ferns in
the gully seemed like funeral plumes, and over the
gully the smoke-like mist floated in heavy clouds,
while through the rain, near and far, showed the
everlasting gums.
; The bride turned from the window with a
Oliver, and crawled back into her bed. It had
tfeen raining for a week and she was anxious, for
well she knew that thirty miles of rough bush-
track and side-cutting, with many flooded creeks,
lay between the selection and the township whence
the clergyman had to be brought.
It was now two hours after the time fixed for the
ceremony. Soon after dawn her mother and
sisters had arrayed her in her bridal attire; and
as they did not think it would be proper for the
guests to see her until just before the wedding,
she had been shut up in her bedroom ever since.
There was no fire-place in the room, and the cold
was so intense that she had passed the greater
part of the time sitting on her bed —her wreath
prevented her lying down. Twenty times
she had rushed to the window at the sound of
wheels, expecting to see the missing groom, but
only to see more sodden guests disgorged from
many-shaped vehicles. As the morning waxed
into afternoon she became hysterical (her imagina-
tion pictured her beloved struggling for his life in
the muddy flood), and it was necessary to
administer brandy and water at short intervals.
At last her straining eyes were rewarded, for as
the rain cleared off for a moment, she discerned a
something—a small object between two larger
ones, followed by another small object far up on
the cutting. That first small object her beating
heart told her was the missing bridegroom ; then
the rain hid him from her sight. Through the
slush he came—a moving lump of mud leading the
two buggy horses, while behind followed another
animated mud-lump, who, with an umbrella,
endeavoured to shelter what had once been a
white paper parcel. They had left the buggy
bogged ten miles away, and had done the
remainder of the journey on foot.
They were supplied with fresh clothes; the
surplice was dried, and at four o’clock the wed-
ding-ceremony took place in the large barn, which
had been festooned with ferns and adorned with
Chinese lanterns and banners. The banners bore
in parti-coloured paper-letters the wish, “ May
God bless their union !” and other appropriate
aspirations. The bride was not nervous, but the
bridegroom was, and in the act of placing the ring
upon her finger he let it fall. There w»-re only two
or three cracks in the barn floor, but the ring dis-
appeared down one of them, and the marriage was
delayed while the youngest brother of the bride
crawled under the barn to recover it.
This being accomplished, the ceremony was
finished satisfactorily. The sister and mother
of the bridegroom were weeping copiously, but
the bride’s relatives seemed rather pleased than
otherwise. One of her brothers confided to a
guest in an audible whisper that “if Billy knew
what a temper she’s got he wouldn’t have been in
such a hurry to git her !” Billy evidently had no
idea of what the future held in store for him, for
a few minutes later helooked perfectly happy seated
at the head of the table with his bride, who had
developed a keen appetite which, with the assist-
ance of half a chicken and some tongue, she was
endeavouring to allay. During the breakfast a
parcel was handed to the bride. It was opened
and found to contain a pair of tiny woollen shoes.
The bride, who had passed the bashful age, handed
them to the bridegroom, who pocketed them with
the remark that he “ hoped they would come in
handy.”
At half-past seven the barn was cleared for
dancing. The band consisted of an aocordeon and
violin. What can the “upper sucoles,” who
change their waltz-step as they change their dress
fashions, know of the beauty of the waltz? No
sooner do they become accustomed to one step
than it is changed for another. Here some of the
men wore medals as a sign of their prowess in the
art terpsichorean. Champion waltzers were they,
dancing the same steps as their fathers did before
them. No rapid moving up and down, in and
out, and reversing, to the imminent danger of
their neighbours. Their evident object was to
gyrate on as small a surface as possible, and keep
an exact distance between themselves and the
couple directlyin front.
, At ten, supper was announced, and afterwards
dancing was renewed with fresh vigour. The rain
still fell steadily, and from across the ranges occa-
sionally came the sullen roar of some giant gum
tearing its roots from the wet earth and crashing
headlong. The bridegroom is dancing merrily;
but the bride is missing. She is found sitting
before a fire, nursing someone’s baby while the
mother dances. She looks aged and weary; her
hair is out of curl, three or four little straight
black wisps hang on each side of her face ; her
wreath is awry—all the orange-blossoms have
fallen off ; there is a little droop at the corner of
her mouth, and there are lines under her eyes.
The unceasing rain is depressing. It is impos-
sible for any of the guests to leave before daylight,
so the bride retires to the nuptial chamber,
accompanied by someone’s baby and the mother
or three other female friends who are to
share her bed. The happy bridegroom makes him-
self as comfortable as he can under the circum-
stances with three chairs, a blue blanket, and his
pipe. He is lulled to sleep by the sound of the
rain beating steadily on the shingles overhead,
instead of by the cooing of his new-made bride.
THE PAUPER.
The Waiter's Lament.—A Boom-Bust
Lay.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I remember, I remember, not many years ago,
When everything was booming and tire champagne
used to flow,
How customers came flocking in and couldn’t find
a seat,
And ordered goose and apple-sauce and wouldn’t
look at meat,
They’d tumble up at half-past twelve and keep it
up till three ;
The waiters was a-buzzin round as busy as could
be—
Roast duck fur one, green peas fur two, and soups
three, a la Reine ;
And, waiter, don't forget to bring a bottle of
champagne,
And put it in a silver jug, and mind you make it
nice—
Four glasses, please, a lemon squeeze, and don’t
forget the ice. ■
Here, waiter, wots’ the best you’ve got upon the
bill of fare ?
Trot us in just wot you like, we’ll leave it in your
care;
A bottle of the finest wine that you can ferret out,
And, waiter, bring the bill to me, to-day I’m
going to shout.
And now the fun is at its height, the waiters shout
and bawl,
The cooks with carving-knife and fork upon the
poultry fall,
While the boss he is a-swearin enough to make
him bust,
And the coat-tails of the waiters, why you can’t
see ’em for dust,
Then the clitter and the clatter that the plates and
dishes make,
And the yellin’ of the mean ones fur their
bloomin’ chops or steak,
Which we don’t take no heed, in course, but let
them yell like mad
Till the people settin’ opposite thinks they’ve
been taken bad.
Those were the bloomin’, boomin’ days when the
bosses used to smile.
And the waiters after luncheon was a-countin’ of
their pile ;
When the pockets of their trousers, afore they
went to bed,
Was a-hangin’ down their bloomin’ legs, just like
two lumps of lead ;
But, Lor’, since then how things have changed in
this hero marv’llous town ;
It’s hard to get a tanner now, let alone a ’arf a
crown,
And if they gives you sixpence for a brushin’ of
their hat
They stays away a month or two and whips the
bloomin’ oat;
And where are now the jolly dogs that used to
roll up stairs
And talk about the land they’d sold, and how the
tramway shares
Had doubled since the morning, which at that
time wasn’t strange,
Then order in a bottle, and say “ Waiter, keep k, » e
change !”
Well, some are on their uppers now, and some are
up the spout
A-doin’ ten and fifteen years afore they will g< t
out,
And some have blown their brains out, arid some
have drowned their woes
In the bottom of the Yarra where the dirty water
flows,
And some have gone to Heaven, and some have
gone to—well,
The Argentine Republic, which is one remove
from hell;
At least that’s what they tells me, I ain’t been
there myself,
And if things go on as they are now, I'll soon be
on the shelf,
For what with cuttin’ down yer screw and tips so

friend who calls. John Hatch, Great Western Vine-
yards Depot, 285 Oollins-street.
Had he been born a day later he would have had
but fifteen birthdays in all his life.
The eternal question concerning the Aus-
tralian accent, and how it originated, and how it
is to be cured, and whether there is any Australian
accent at all, has branched out into many new
directions. The Australian grammar is now the
subject principally in dispute. The latest writer
—who calls himself “Australian Native”—com-
plains :
For instance, “deceased left a wife” has occurred
frequently in the columns of the leading daily, when it
should have been—deceased left or leaves a widow. Why,
even in the State School First Reader, the children read,
“ A widow is a woman whose husband is dead.’’
This is getting things down tolerably fine. For,
as it happens, deceased doesn’t leave a widow.
A woman is not a widow until she is left by her
husband, and the husband can’t leave what he has
left already. And it is also questionable if he
leaves a wife, for she ceases to be a wife the
instant he leaves her. The truly grammatical
inwardness of the situation seems to be that he
leaves Mrs. Smith and her family. This is the
only relationship which is permanent; providing
the deceased’s name was Smith the lady was Mrs.
Smith before his death, and she continues so after-
wards. Grammar is an interesting subject when
you get fairly into it.
We desire to mention, for the benefit of
readers of The Bulletin, that Hordern Brothers,
the well-known drapers of 203 Pitt-st., are sending
(post free) anything you order to any part of
N S.W.. Victoria or Queensland ; it would be as
well, when you write, to mention to them that
you heard of it first through this paper. This
firm has been known for years as reliable and
doing a steady business, but of late they have
struck out in an extraordinarily vigorous style,
sending parcels everywhere free, &0., &o.*

Tales of the Austral Tropics,
Says the celebrated author of “ Robbery Under
Arms”“ In these Tales of the Austral Tropics
will be found the strange romances which write
themselves, often in letters of blood, amid the
half-unknown mysterious regions of tropical Aus-
tralia. That they are not less true than terrible
I take it upon myself to affirm.
“ The writer has turned scene-painter for the
nonce and limned with strongest lifelike effect the
Drama of the Waste. ”

be added to that of the succeeding com-
petition.

by post-office order or stamps of any pro-
vince ; when the latter are sent, a penny
to be added for exchange.
